O, my children, now I complete this book for you, which is the last of the science of medicine; and by its completion at the end, and by its explanations, and by its clarifications, it seems to me that I make clear to you whom I love this treatise, which is part of the operation as I practice it, namely surgery.
For the operation as I practice it is almost entirely lacking in our region and in our time; moreover, the science of it has become scarce, and its practice has ceased. Except for some small descriptions in the books of the Ancients, which have altered much and led to those errors and hesitation where the intentions of it are misunderstood and the force of it weakened and the art lost.
And it seems to me that I should enliven this with an orderly arrangement of this treatise according to the way of explanation and clarification and abbreviation; and so that it comes with the forms of cautery tools and other instruments of the work as is through addition of clarification, and preparation for it, which cause is necessary.
But the reason why a good artisan is not found in this my craft in our time is that the art of medicine is long, and it requires the practitioner to be exercised in the science of anatomy, which includes knowing the functions of the members and their forms, as well as their conjunctions and separations; having knowledge of the bones, nerves, and muscles, and their numbers and arrangement; and of the pulsating veins and quiet ones, and the places for the incision of them.
And according to Hippocrates it is said that in name it is small, but in the work it is little, and especially in the art of the hand.
But we have already said this in the introduction to this book; for whoever is not knowledgeable about what we have said about anatomy will not be safe from error by which men are killed, as I have seen too many of them, for some are formed in this science and throw themselves into it without that knowledge and experience.
For I saw a mad physician who thought he had incised an abscess in the neck of a woman, and he cut some arteries in the neck so that much blood flowed out, causing the woman to die quickly.
And I saw another physician who thought he would remove a stone from a man who had been suffering for some time, and the stone was large, and he came to him and extracted that stone along with part of the bladder, whereupon that man died on the third day.
And I had already called to extract that stone and saw that because of the size of the stone and the condition of the patient, I knew beyond a doubt that he would die.
And I saw another physician who was paid wages by a duke of our land to perform surgery on a castrated black man who had a fracture in his thigh; and due to the physician's ignorance, he exposed the fracture above the wound with splints and bandages in a strong structure, but did not leave room for drainage.
And he untied it according to his desires, and soon after he bandaged it again, and after some days he commanded that we untie the bandage; and thus it remained until the thigh and leg became abscessed, leading to his ruin.
Thus he calls out to him, and having prepared himself he loosens the bandage, and once calm is achieved he begins to relieve his pains.
However, if corruption was confirmed in the limb, so that it could not be restrained, and it did not cease to spread the corruption throughout the limb until the patient was in danger.
And I saw another surgeon who incised a cancerous abscess, and it became ulcerated after a few days, until the malignancy of his work was greatly revealed to him by his lord.
The reason for this is that the cancerous condition is pure of melancholic humor; therefore, it does not need to be treated with iron at all, but rather in the member which suffers and is accustomed to such treatment.
And for this reason, children, it is necessary for you that the operation be planned in two ways: one is an operation associated with health, and the other is work in which there is risk even under the best conditions.
And I have already stated this in every place in this book where an operation involves both error and fear; therefore, it is necessary for you to heed this and leave that, so that the pages do not find themselves without a way to speak and thereby dishonor you.
And so take up your arms with diligence and the protection of God, and your patients with skill and firmness.
And use the best method by God's grace to lead to health and praiseworthy success, and leave behind the terrible illnesses that are difficult to heal, and avoid those things that make you fear, so that doubt does not undermine your faith and reputation: for it is better for you to preserve your glory, and more so in this world and hereafter for your sanctity.
Wound treatment in some monastic surgeries: Do not treat illness badly, for thus bad doctors are not named.
And I have devoted this book according to three chapters.
Begin the division of the book, and first of the first part. The first chapter is ordered about the head and neck, in the forms of instruments and in the tools of cautery, and of what is necessary for the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, about incisions and punctures, and about suction cups, and bandages, and the extraction of arrows and similar instruments, fully chaptered and ordered in the forms of instruments.
The third chapter, about restoration and dislocation, and the treatment of twisting and similar conditions; a chapter ordered about the head and neck, and the forms of instruments.
I will describe the quality of the movements of that cautery and its necessities, and in which condition cautery is prohibited.
Therefore, I say that the speech about the quality of the movements of cauterization and its necessities is long and extensive, subtle by science, and hidden as a secret; and many wise people have spoken much about it, and they are discordant about it, but for me a little of their speeches suffices to avoid prolongation.
Therefore, I say that cauteries should be applied to the whole body universally, whether it is made of matter or not, except for two parts, which are tail-like parts without matter.
In the tail-like and dry part made of matter, it dries out; for some say that cautery is beneficial for this, while others say the opposite, that cautery is not suitable for a disease caused by quality and dryness, because the nature of fire is hot and dry, and it is unsuitable to heal a tail-like and dry part with what is hot and dry.
And let the one who says the opposite consider this: that cautery is beneficial for every tail-like and dry disease that occurs in human bodies, because when you compare the human body and its humidity to the nature of fire, you find that the human body is more fragile.
And I say by the words of that person, that experience has already shown this to me on several occasions.
But I do not wish to impose this on one who has already been tested and exercised in the chapter of cautery by previous practice, and who has known the diversity of human constitution and the disposition of diseases in their seasons, and the causes and accidents of them, and the time span of those.
But in the other parts, there is no fear for you, especially in fragile and humid diseases, because all physicians are now in agreement about those, and they do not disagree on the benefit of cautery in such cases.
And you know, my children, that the secret of healing by cautery with fire and its goodness lies in the fact that fire is simple, and its action affects only the member being cauterized and does not harm other members nearby except for slight damage.
Here is the translation:
May the fire, by its sublimity and goodness of substance, do nothing but superfluify. And it is known to us through experiment and length of service in studying this art about the truth of causes; and thereby I excuse myself from the length of speech. And if not that it were not read in my book in this pause would I hide from you secrets with fire, and the quality of its operation on bodies and expulsion thereof by which you expel diseases, in a philosophical demonstrative speech, which would be sharp in your ears.
And know, children, that they err who disagree about the time when it is convenient to perform cautery and pause at the best time of spring.
And I say that cautery is convenient at all times, for the harm caused by accidental circumstances of time is submerged in the benefit brought about by what is drawn from cautery, and even more so if the cautery is necessary for pains or necessary to alleviate severe and urgent conditions, which cannot endure delay due to fear of the malice that might result, since it is better to have minor harm enter at a bad time than to suffer greater ill.
And this I do not say in your ears, or, children, what only the common people and foolish physicians believe is that for the illness which is cured by cautery there is not a suitable time always, and they pause at that consequence.
And the reason is not as such that they think, for cautery is nothing but a substitute for medicine which alters the complexion and dries up the humidities, which are the cause of pains.
But nevertheless, cautery surpasses medicine in speed due to the value and virtue of its operation and the greatness of its efficacy.
It is possible that his illness may return at some time according to the condition of the patient and the location of his illness, and its nature, and what has been prepared in his body by the accumulation of superfluities within him, and what has reached him through its acquisition from food and similar causes; but if the illness for which cauterization was administered is a subtle illness in a limb with little superfluity and moisture, such as the cauterization of a tooth for pain and similar cases, it is possible that that pain will not return to him, but this is done rarely.
And the common saying is that cauterization is the last remedy of medicine, and it is said by practitioners, not because those who understand it think there is no cure in medicine or any other cause before cauterization.
And the cause is contrary to that; and the understanding that cauterization is the last remedy of medicine is only when we administer methods of treatment for certain illnesses, and those medicines do not work, and then at the end of the cause cauterization is administered, and it proves effective. And here in what has been said so far that cauterization is the last remedy of medicine, not according to the understanding which I understand of the common misconceptions and errors of foolish doctors.
And the ancients said that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron, and they did not say this except for the equality of gold and the sublimity of its substance.
And they said that the location for cauterization does not matter, which is not absolutely true, because I have experienced this and have found that it only works in some bodies but not others, and cauterization with gold is good and better than iron as they say; however, when you use fire to cauterize with gold, it is not clear to you how to do it properly, since the hardness of gold requires a certain quantity for ignition, and it prepares to receive friction. If you adjust the heat over it by fire, the gold will melt due to the heat, and if you place some sheets in the fire, the craftsman can then use them for that purpose.
And thus is made the cautery of iron, which should be applied gently and gradually according to the correctness of the procedure.
And I have already arranged this chapter on cautery according to the chapters, and I have placed those of the head among the parts, so that I relieve the inquirer who seeks what he wants from it.
The first chapter.
This cautery is applied to the cold and humidity that cause headaches from which these two causes originate: they are the cause of soreness and a multitude of rheumatic fluxes called corizza, affecting the head and parts of the face and ears, as well as excessive sleepiness, toothache, and gout, and generally all illnesses caused by cold, such as paralysis, apoplexy, and similar conditions.
The form of this cautery is as follows: first, command the patient to be evacuated with a laxative medicine that cleanses the head for three or four nights, according to the strength of the patient and their age and habit.
And then command him to shave his head with a razor, and then place him seated on a padded bench with his arms resting on his own knees.
And then place the palm of your hand on the forehead above the root of the nose between his eyebrows. But when the heat of your middle finger has reached it, mark that place with ink, and then apply the olive wood cautery, which has this form:
And then pause there at the marked place with the probe, in such a way that it descends, by which the tissue may be gradually compressed, and you rotate it.
And then lift your hand gently and look at the place, and if you see any amount of the head bone already uncovered, as much as the amount of grain in a bushel, then raise your hand, and if not return your hand to that tool, or another like it, if it is cooled, until you can see the bone which I have told you about.
And then take a little salt and dissolve it on the place and let it be for three days.
And then pause on the embedded cautery and let it be inside until the burn scar is destroyed. And then treat the place with a quadruple ointment until it is healed.
And it is said that when the wound has become uncovered, the sooner you treat the arm, the better and more beneficial it is.
And some of them say that the wound should be cauterized down to the bone, and the cautery should be held until it adheres to some part of the bone.
And then remove what is adhered to the bone, and then treat it.
And others say that with the cautery it should reach to the deepest point, until it presses strongly on the bone until the bone melts, and this is the method of alpechini (cautery in small degrees): And it is believed that vapors from the head emanate from that place.
And let the wound be treated for a long time until it is healed and has become firm.
I have not seen these two species of cautery used on everyone, but only on some people out of fear; and leave that one alone, for me it is better to heal with salves, when possible; for the head is easily damaged by breaking its continuity in this way as we have witnessed in other parts of the body, especially when the patient's constitution is cold.
And the first species of cautery is safer and better for me; use that one with salves.
Chapter Second.
Of cautery of the head.
When chronic and long-lasting pain occurs throughout the head, and it has been prolonged in the patient through the use of various remedies such as plasters, compresses, caps, poultices, ointments, cataplasms, cauteries, and similar treatments, and especially if one cautery has already been applied without any improvement from those causes, then consider whether the patient's head is of strong constitution and not cold, and if there is much pain, apply another cautery after that one for a little while. And then cauterize that area above the crown of their head with cautery until the pain is relieved. Cut open the affected area and expose the bone in the amount we have described, and cauterize that with a cautery at the back of the head where it is called "vas" — which is the place where the windpipe is located. And approach it thus, and do not expose the bone, for the patient will experience great pain different from the pain of all other cauteries of the head.
And I shall describe this cautery in its place, for it is more subtle than the one used to cauterize the horns of the head and the occiput; and this is the form of it.
Chapter Three.
On Cauterization of Recent Hemorrhoids.
When intense pain is found in the part of the head, or if there is lingering pain within the skull, it should be evacuated with medicines that cleanse the head, and another treatment should be administered, which I have described in the sections on diseases; but if this does not benefit, then cauterization is to be performed in two ways: either by cauterizing with a sharp combustible medicine or with iron.
But if you perform the cautery with a combustible medicine, take a piece of cloth, burn it, and hold it by both ends. And then incise the area of the pain on the temple with a lancet until it reaches just below the skin, so that it is hidden.
And then press on it with a pad for about an hour and leave it there for three hours; after that, remove the pad and apply a poultice for two or three days, and afterward place a compress soaked in honey until the area becomes warm.
And then care for it with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, use with some medicines for burning which I have written in the second chapter about burning medicines.
And this is how you perform cautery with an instrument made of iron, which has the following form:
and it is called a clamp with a nail head, because the top of it resembles the shape of a nail head, which has some curvature, and in the middle part there is a small protrusion.
And then place it on the location of the pain, and hold your hand — so that the artery below is not adhered to — and rotate the iron slowly.
And if the amount that is adhered due to the curvature is as much as half of it, raise your hand so that the artery below is not adhered to, so that there is no flow of blood.
And then soak cotton in salt water and place it on the area, and leave it for three days; and then place dry cotton over it, and subsequently treat it with an ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, perform cautery at the end of the radial bone according to the cautery method, and be careful not to cut the artery in this proper but non-traditional procedure.
Chapter Four.
On Cauterization of the Ancient Artery.
When you treat the artery as we have described in the previous treatment and in the sections on diseases, and if the treatment does not improve, and sometimes due to the disease it does not suffice with what we have said about the initial cautery with medicine or the cautery with fire, then it is necessary for you to ignite the radial cautery until it becomes white-hot. Place it along the site of pain in a line that is half the length of your finger or slightly more, and then remove your hand quickly so as not to cut the artery and penetrate to the bone.
However, it is necessary for you to guard against the continuity of the jawbone, which affects chewing, and that the lizard [likely referring to a muscle or structure] is not adhered and the nerves which move it, so that no spasm occurs.
And if it is about the custody and caution of the flow of blood from the artery that is cut, for in its cutting there is fear, and especially for one who does not know what he is doing and is not skilled and experienced; and leave the operation to him at the beginning.
And here comes as a reminder of the management of blood flow in cases of arterial injury according to the usual method, in this place of this book.
And if you see in this illness that these cauteries are not sufficient and the patient's condition is tolerable, cauterize at the appropriate place on the head as we have recounted, and treat the wound until it is healed.
And if you perform the cauteries that we have mentioned in the chapter on the rupture of arteries with a cautery having two edges, this is better and more effective.
Chapter 5.
Of the cautery for ear pains.
When pain in the ear occurs due to friction and is treated with laxative causes and the other treatment that I have mentioned in the division, and the pain does not improve, now use the cautery, and this one has the name puncta, and here is its form:
And approach the puncta to the fire, and move it around the entire ear as a wheel turns, or in the vicinity of each part if the pain is in that part. Take the cautery of the hair removal from the ear little by little until you have marked the place with it.
And if the cautery reaches those points throughout the ear, and wherever it does, take care of the places until they are healed.

And place your cautery on the opposite side of the diseased part, because softening occurs only in the part that appears healthy.
The method of cautery is that you cauterize there with the cautery on the right side of the uppermost part of the earlobe, and a little above the horn of the head, and in another place in the temple area, the length of which should be according to the length of your thumb, and then hold your hand with the cautery until half the quantity of the root is burned.

Chapter VII.
On cauterizing for chronic apoplexy.
When apoplexy is chronic and you have treated it with the methods we have mentioned and your treatment does not induce fever in the patient, then you should cauterize at four points: above all the temples of the head; one point on the middle part of the forehead, as we have said; and one point on the nape of the neck. And the method for the cauterization is done as mentioned above.
And it is also cauterized above the mouth of the stomach; this is better done later; and then proceed with the treatment as appropriate.
Chapter VIII.
On cauterizing for forgetfulness caused by phlegm.
In the beginning, give the patient large draughts and pills made from substances that purify and cleanse.
And then shave the patient's head completely and apply a plaster to the back of the neck using the recipe described in the chapter on plasters.
And apply this for several applications, as this is the method of cauterization. And do this according to the order of the method that I have already mentioned.
And if the patient improves with these treatments; if not, then perform three cauterizations in the rear part of the head, which are located descending from the highest part of the head to the part closest to the neck. Between each cautery, wait until the swelling of the finger subsides, and then proceed with the treatment using water.
If you are accustomed to the pain and can tolerate it, then proceed with the cautery in its proper place; and if you require more intensity, then apply the cautery over both sides.
Proceed with the treatment until it is healed; and perform the olive-shaped cautery as described above.
Chapter IX.
On the cautery for parasites and the softening of the entire body.
Proceed with the cleansing of the head using scissors and water as we have described; then shave the patient's head, and proceed to apply the cautery in its proper place on the head, over all sides of the head, and at the back part of it, and three cauteries along the vertebrae of the neck.
And if it is necessary for the illness of softening the body to apply that treatment and the patient can tolerate it and the illness is severe, then proceed with four cauteries along the vertebrae of the back, and finally apply a cautery near the armpit, and raise the temperature gradually. Proceed with the treatment until it is healed, and this should be an olive-shaped cautery.
Chapter X.
On the cautery for epilepsy.
An epileptic should not be cauterized except when the epilepsy is due to phlegm.
Therefore, mix the brain at the beginning of the year and during the other treatment which we have mentioned in the division when the patient is large and can tolerate the exception of the medicines.
And if it is a child who cannot tolerate the medicines, then administer gargle and mouthwash made from mixed brain, for several days before that with the improvement of food.
And then shave the patient's head, and immediately cauterize that area with a cautery in the middle part of the head according to the previous method, and with another cautery on the occipital region, and on each side of the head apply cauterization. And if the patient is strong and can tolerate it, then cauterize that area with the cauteries which I have described, having paralysis and relaxation of the body over the cervical vertebrae and the thoracic vertebrae.
And let it be an olive pit cautery as was previously described.
But if the patient is a child, then use a gentle cautery according to this method.
Chapter .xi.
On Cauterization for Melancholia.
When the causes of melancholia are due to corrupted and flaccid humors, then cauterize that area with the cauteries which we have described, having paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholia is an excess inclining towards melancholia, and the patient's body is humid, then give them to drink what purifies their brain according to what was mentioned previously in the division.
Cut off the top of the diseased part, and then carefully place a suitable bandage around the circumference, and then position it in the middle of the head.
And if the diseased part is six inches wide, it should be held from all sides.
And then apply an ointment; and it is more suitable to use one made from sheep's wool. And warm that on the fire with moderate heat as needed, which the patient can tolerate when applied. And then place that in the middle of the head around it in a circle, and let it remain so that it can be rubbed.
And do this with the diseased part once a week, and follow another regimen until it is healed; or if you prefer, cauterize that in small points as needed, but make sure to hold the hand properly when using cautery: this is a special type of cautery that moistens the brain with moderation.
And then place that cloth soaked in wool or goose fat.

On the cautery of old wounds.

Chapter XIV.
On the cautery for nasal odor.

Chapter XV.
On Cauterization for Softening the Eyelids of the Eyes.
When the eyelid of the eye is softened due to illness or humidity, then cauterize that eyelid with this lunar-shaped cautery; and this is its form:

Chapter XVI.
On Cauterization of the Eyelid When the Hairs Turn Inside and Scratch the Eye.
When the hairs of the eyelid turn inward and scratch the eye, cauterization should be done in two ways: either by performing it or using a burning medication.
If the cautery is done with fire, instruct the patient beforehand to cut their hairs as those of ordinary people are cut, such that they are extended and even; and if they scratch from their origin, then you should bandage the eyes so that it does not happen again until the hairs grow back.
When a wound or ulcer appears, then place the head of the patient in such a way that one marks above the eyelids with some sign, which is according to the shape of a leaf of mercury, and let the beginning of the mark be near the hair.
And then place a probe into the eye socket or in mucus of dust on the eye.
And then prepare the cautery, which has this form.
And then cauterize over the figure you have marked, little by little, in small stages, until the surface of the wound which is like a leaf of myrtle is completely burned properly.
And the sign of health in this work is that you see the eyelid of the eye already contracted and the hair of it raised up near the puncture of the eye.
And then raise your hand and lay it aside for three days, and then place the probe on the border until the scar is eradicated. And then care for that with herbs until it is healed.
But if the hair around the area regrows and the eyelid is softened, then perform the cautery on that again just as you did initially.
But if the hair is below the eyelid, then cauterize that until it rises to its natural place and becomes even so that the hair does not touch the eye.
But the cautery with a burning medicine is that you instruct the patient to stretch out the hair until it is extended and even, and then make it in the shape of a leaf of myrtle from paper or cloth.
Take soap known and live lard one unc. i, or as much water, and quickly mix those causes well, and make that without delay so that it is not chilled; and then spread that liquid over the sheet which is made in the shape of a myrtle leaf, and place that on both wounds; and place underneath the poultice covered in white cloth, and let the head of the patient be towards you.
And place the hand on the medicine and press it slightly so that the patient feels the stinging of the medicine, for there is a burning in it like fire.
And as long as the burning sensation persists, leave the medicine on and move it with your hand; when the stinging stops, then remove the medicine and wash the wound with water; and then check if the eyelid is raised as it was washed, in which case you should apply constriction with heat or incision, for that is good; but if not, return to applying the medicine over the place where it has not been applied yet until it darkens before your operation is complete and the wound is constricted.
And then place over the wound with borders until the root of the injury is eradicated, and then care for it with a defined remedy or another until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you to guard with vigilant diligence that no dust from the medicine falls into the wound, and if the eyelid after some days becomes softened and some cause of hair growth appears in the wound, then reapply your medicine to that place where the eyelid is softened, and properly as you did at the beginning, and then around it until it is healed.
This is the form of the myrtle leaf.
And you should know that the leaves of men are diverse in size, and therefore according to this your contraction is made.
And the way of rectitude will not be hidden from one who studies in this art.
Chapter XVII.
On cauterization of a fistula that is in the tear duct of the eye.
When you treat a fistula as we have said in the divisions of diseases and do not confer with your treatment, then it is necessary that you cauterize it according to this method: instruct the patient to place his head between your thighs, and hold his head still by someone else's hand between your hands in such a way that it does not move or shake.
And then place a cloth in egg white or on a pad of fine feathers over his eye.
And then light the cautery, which has this form:
which should be covered, as in the shape of a reed or an eagle feather, at one end of which the cautery is made.
And if you wish, it should penetrate into the other end. And if you want it to be like a thread, like a tentacle.
But the one that is covered is better for your work.
If you approach treating the fistula when it is suppurated, then drain it and dry it out. And if it is not suppurated, then open it and extract the putrefaction from it.
And if you proceed with the cautery, make sure it is well heated. Hold it at a distance but not too far from the nose so that your hand does not tremble or cause harm, and then place the cautery on the gums and burn it.
And so if at the beginning of your cautery you perceive it entering the bone, that is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice as needed. Then leave it for three days, and afterwards place a bandage around it and apply a drying agent until it is healed.
But if after these days it has not healed, then apply an acute corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed and proceed as appropriate according to its chapter.
And there is another method of cauterizing a fistula, which the ancients first described: Go to the location of the fistula and open it, then place in that fissure a thin piece whose shape fits it. And put in there one ounce of molten lead; hold your hand gently but firmly, so that the injury is not aggravated because the molten lead does not reach the tissue around it. You should also place over the injured area a poultice made from egg white or water, since the lead adheres to the location of the fistula and heals it miraculously.
And if the fistula is healed with what we have said about the cautery and treatment, that is good; but if not, then it is necessary to administer nasal irrigation and reduce the fistula to its proper place within the nose.

Chapter Nineteen.
On cauterizing a fistula in the mouth.
When an abscess occurs on the uvula or palate or at the roots of the teeth, and it bursts, becomes ruptured, and then deteriorates to form a fistula, and when you treat this and it does not heal effectively, it is necessary for you to cauterize with fire according to the size of the fistula. Chapter XX.
On cautery for teeth and on softening the gums.
When the gums are softened by moisture and the teeth are decayed, then apply these medicines and do not confine them. Place the head of the patient in a suitable position, and proceed with cautery in the following manner: place the cautery over the tooth and insert it into the area to be cauterized quickly while it is still heated, but do not let it touch the gum near the root of the tooth where the heat can affect the surrounding tissue.
And then remove your hand, and proceed to repeat the cautery several times as you wish.
And then the patient should rinse their mouth with salt water and hold it for one hour, after which they may spit it out, as the decayed tooth is now stabilized, the softened gum is strengthened, and the corrupted moisture is dried up.
Chapter XXI.
On cautery for toothache.
When toothache is due to coldness or there is decay or inflammation and the treatment with medicine does not confine it, then cautery in that case is done in two ways: either with a cow's horn or with fire.
Cautery with a horn is when you take a cow's horn and heat it over charcoal, then take cloth and wrap it around the end of the horn. Proceed to immerse the heated horn in water briefly, and quickly place that on the painful tooth until it is cooled.
From there, apply some burns until the heat of the fire reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you do, soak a piece of wool or cloth in the aforementioned broth, and place it on the painful tooth, and put an ignited iron on top until the iron reaches the gum around the tooth.
However, for cautery with fire, you should use the tip of an iron or steel instrument, and place it in the center of any swelling so that the intensity of the heat does not reach the mouth of the patient.
And then ignite the cautery, which will be in a specific form, and place it on the tooth with your hand until the cautery is thoroughly rubbed; do this several times until the pain subsides either on that day or the next.
And it is necessary that the patient rinse their mouth with good broth due to the pressure of that cautery and hold it in for an hour, then spit it out; this is the form of the cautery.
And cauterize as much as you wish according to what is possible for you.

And then care for that which was burnt and bandage it with cloths until it is healed.

Chapter XXVII.
On the cautery of the afflicted groin.
When pain arises in the groin due to coldness or moisture or excessive flatulence causing deviation from its normal condition by excessive evacuation, and it is treated with what we have mentioned without compression, then the patient should be placed on his back with his head raised, and you should apply three cauterizations according to this form and quantity, placing them over the groin below the hypochondria, as a person lies.

Chapter XXVIII.
On the piercing of an abscess of the testicle with a cautery.
When you find an inflammation or abscess in the testicle and want to know whether that abscess is in the flesh of the testicle or in its tunica, if it is in the flesh of the testicle, the pain will be heavy and severe.
And if it is in the tunica of the testicle, the pain will be sharp and intense, and often fatigues the physicians who treat it.
Therefore, you should position the patient on his back; and then feel the location of the abscess by touching it; and then use the cautery that resembles a reed, the form of which was described on the previous page. And cauterize with that cautery until the entire poultice is consumed, until it reaches the center, until healthy tissue appears.
And then treat that area as you would for other abscesses until it is healed.
And it is not appropriate to use this method of cautery except for someone whose study in the art of medicine is extensive, and who has knowledge of many illnesses; but even then, one should attempt this experiment only a few times before daring to perform such an operation, but rather leave it to those more experienced, which is better.
Chapter XXIX.
On Cauterizing Pustules.
Thus say the ancients about cauterizing pustules: It should be done according to this method. Take a thin slice of the root of leek (aristology in medieval text often refers to plants like garlic or leek), which is as long as your finger and as thick as your little finger, from dried roots.
And then submerge it in oil and heat it over the fire. Cauterize with it at a point between the clavicle and the neck; and two small cauteries below that, slightly inclined towards the part under the beard; and two more slightly inclined between the fifth rib and the kidney, inclining slightly towards the back parts; and another cauterization in the middle of the legs; and one above the stomach; and three on the rear parts; and one at a point between the two shoulders; and two at the sides of the buttocks below the cauterization between the shoulders.
And do not cover it with your hand during the cauterization, but leave it exposed.
And some ancient authors also say that for some men who use a cauterization with iron resembling a blade, one should heat it and apply it to the point between the ribs so as to reach the abscess and cause it to discharge pus, as we have said regarding the abscess of the testicle.
But in this perforation with the cautery there is no fear, for it often happens that one dies immediately or an accident occurs at a place where healing is not possible.
Chapter XXX.
On the cautery of the Melesa.
When you treat the disease of Melesa as we have said about its cure in the division and do not confer with your treatment, now you should cauterize according to three ways, all of which are appropriate. One way is that you make three or four linear cauterizations according to the length of the Melesa, following the figure of the cautery used for the fetus, the form of which has been described previously.
And there should be a space between each cautery equal to the thickness of a finger or a little more, and the method of the cautery should be the same method we have described for the cautery of the fetus.
And do not compress your hand during the cautery; and let the disease be above the skin.
And there is another method of cauterizing, which is to ignite the cautery vertically in two places, which cause we have described in the chapter on the dislocation of the muscle, and raise the shoulder, which is on the right side of the Melesa where the pain of the disease occurs.
And let your elevation be such that when you raise the shoulder according to the length of the body, the cauterizations are made according to the width of the body.
And then insert both ends, ignite one end, and at that point where it penetrates the shoulder on the other side.
And then remove the cautery; and thus four cauterizations are completed.
And if you want to cauterize, have three burnings prepared.
And if your cauterization is such that it causes pain so severe that the patient cannot move their arm for several days, know that this may be more effective than other preceding methods in treatment.

And know that when you do not have the bone with the cautery, do not proceed with your operation.
And it is appropriate that the cautery for children be gentle according to their age and size.
And afterwards, take care of the site of the cautery for three days with a dressing until the burn scar has healed.
And afterwards, treat that with other ointments until it is healed.
And let the patient lie on their back for forty days until the site is sealed and the condition healed.
And it is appropriate to restrict the patient's diet according to the duration of the healing period during which the intestines are weakened, so that the bowel does not protrude due to excessive effort.
And afterwards, when you want to remove it after forty days, administer it carefully and properly, and keep that for another forty days.
And let them be restricted from work and from overeating food and drink, and from speaking loudly, for when they follow this regimen of complete healing, they will be healed.

and it is marked with a line that you follow along the latitude in the groin above the rupture, and another line below; then place your hand with a nail-shaped cautery, but the first cautery is quicker and better.
Chapter XLVI.
On the Cauterization of Torticollis.
When pain occurs in any members due to falling or striking and that pain persists for a long time and does not respond to treatment with medicines, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And the cautery for each member should be according to its size and smallness, and its nature and condition, and the location of the torticollis and the pain in it.
If it heals with the first cautery, that is good; but if not, then repeat the cautery. For it is customary for such pains to be punctured in the member closest to where the pain is, so you should achieve this with the cautery until the patient is healed.
Chapter XLVII.
On the Cauterization of Leprosy.
Some cases of leprosy are greatly helped by cauterization, but rarely when the type of leprosy is caused by the putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy.
And so when you wish to cauterize that person, consider whether the leprosy is commissary and treat it with what we have said in the division and do not cease until this is done, and you fear over the patient that the corruption comprehend the whole body of him and all the constitution of him; then cauterize that person on the head with a cauterizing iron: one at the mid location of the head, and another further down from it, near the forehead to the end of the hair; and both above two horns; and one behind, over the nape or back of the neck.
And finally, your work is to press the cautery a little inside so that it impresses upon the bone a small impression, and thereby displace the scabs, so that the expulsion of thick vapors may be facilitated; and cauterize over the membrane as is necessary.
And if the leprosy has already spread over the patient and appears manifestly, then it is necessary that you cauterize those places with the cauteries which we have mentioned on the head; and with a cautery on the extremity of the nose, and two cauteries on each cheek, and two cauteries on the vertebrae of the neck, and six cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and one large cautery on the vertebrae near the buttocks, and one on the buttocks, and two on the thighs, and one on each thigh, and two on the knees, and one on each knee, and two on the muscles, and two on the tendons of his feet; and cauterize that person over all the joints of the aforementioned arms and legs, and on each heel of his feet, and on the hollows of his hands.
And be careful of the nerves which lie above the heel so that you do not burn them.
In some cases, it is cauterized with a cautery on the bone of the penis and with another on the mouth of the stomach, and another on the spleen.
And know that the more you adjust the cautery, the more beneficial and effective it is; and know that the patient does not feel pain from the cautery as much as the bystanders do, because the body of the patient has already been numbed.
And it is necessary that the cauteries be large or few according to the members and joints, as dictated by the properties of the tools.
And immediately apply the cautery with flour of barley mixed with honey, and continue the other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter XLVIII.
On Cauterization for Stupor.
When a limb is numb or asleep and is treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, but does not heal, then you should cauterize that numb limb with cauteries according to the size of the limb, whether large or small.
And let the heat of the cautery penetrate into the tissue slightly. And then treat that area with an ointment until it is healed.
And in some cases, numbness is cauterized which extends to the muscles and bones, and along the vertebrae of the back, as well as the course of the nerves that move that limb, and the numbness is alleviated.
But do not proceed with this except by one who is wise in the anatomy of limbs and the course of nerves throughout the moving body.
Chapter XLIX.
On cauterizing the parts that are discolored.
When the discolored area is old and does not respond to any of the medicinal ointments, then you should cauterize it. Apply the cautery for a short period, according to the quantity needed until the whiteness and color return to normal, and the color changes accordingly.
And for the treatment, apply a paste made of flour and lentils mixed with rose oil and arnica leaves, and add pigeon blood or swallow blood, using an equal part of each and mixing them together. Then place a cloth on it until it is healed.
Chapter L.
On cauterizing a cancer.
When cancer begins and you want to remove it, then cauterize around it using a circular cautery as it appears.
And the wise say that it should be cauterized with a hot iron in the exact spot; but I have not seen this done, for I fear it will become ulcerated, and I have observed this happening several times.
And it is safer to cauterize around it in a circular manner as we have said, using either a circular or a moxa cautery.
Chapter LI.
On cauterizing of abscesses.
When an abscess is delayed in maturing due to excessive moisture in that area, the poor condition of the patient's health, their advanced age and debility, lack of blood, or because of the time; if you wish to hasten its maturation, then cauterize around it with small moxa cauteries. After this, leave it as it will now mature more quickly.
If you want the perforation of the place to be cauterized, then at that time use fire for the cautery which has this shape; and place it in the middle location until it penetrates the bone.
And if the cautery is applied to that place where there is pus, so that the arm may be relieved; and then take care that it heals inside.
Chapter LII.
On Cauterization for Corrosive Agents.
A corrosive is a wandering corruption in the limb, which corrodes as fire consumes dry wood.
Therefore, if you see the corrosive in a place that can withstand cautery with fire, then use fire to cauterize small and large nails as is appropriate in the place where the corrosion is.
And then cauterize it thoroughly until you eradicate all the corrosion and leave no cause for it to remain.
And then let it rest for three days, and apply thrice a day with oil mixed until all the scars are eradicated and all the corruption is gone.
And then treat it with substances that promote flesh growth.
However, if after three days you see that the flesh grows healthy with no corruption, that is good; but if not, then repeat the cautery on what remains in the corrupted areas.
And some corrosives are treated with sharp medicine, for that is the method in place of cautery. However, a cautery from fire is of little use for healing pus.
And here I have described the cure of acute diseases in the division, and therefore take that one when you feel an onset of illness.
Chapter LIII.
On Cauterization of Joints and Other Similar Areas.
In many cases, near the lower parts below the foot, there is a severe illness called a joint, which is caused by thick and hard substances that affect the foot; and in these cases cauterization is done in two ways: one with fire and the other with hot water.
However, the cautery with fire is made by heating the concave cautery, similar to a lentil in shape, which is made of iron according to the quantity that contains the joint from all sides, and it should be very thin.
And then place that heated iron on the joint, and immediately rotate your hand with the cautery around the circumference of the joint until the cautery reaches the depth of the joint. And leave that for three days until it begins to discharge pus. And then apply a poultice made of crushed wild mugwort and salt to it, and leave the poultice on for one night, as this will help eradicate it from its roots.
And then treat the area with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
And if it does not convert, and in many cases this happens on the surface of the body, especially on the thighs and legs, then you should administer cauterization with hot water. Take a tube made of iron or brass or lentil-shaped metal, and place it on the joint; then pour a small amount of hot water into the tube, hold your hand around it, and rotate it thoroughly using the compression of your hand from all sides, ensuring that the edge of the tube reaches the root of the joint. Allow the hot water to act for a short period due to its scalding effect for about an hour, then remove it, as this will eradicate the joint along with its roots.
And thus place them one near the other until you come upon all of those that are naturally in the body.
And near them, treat those abscesses whose roots are to be eradicated with the applications that cause flesh to grow.
Chapter LIII.
On the cautery for trembling.
When you encounter trembling due to irritation of the nerves or from a fever that causes irritation, or from some similar cause, it is then appropriate for you to cauterize that area with four cauteries or five on the vertebrae of the back, between each vertebra one caustic; and at the feet of the patient, and over the stomach, use an olive-shaped caustic.
And the trembling of the patient subsides and hastens the recovery from the illness that causes irritation.
Chapter LV.
On the cautery for accidental injuries to the body.
When there appear on the body accidental swellings and pus-filled wounds that are formed from thick, corrupted matter; then at the beginning when they appear, it is appropriate to cauterize over the entire fistula with a fine cautery made of myrtle wood, the end of which is extended over fire; or with the root of long erestologia; or with a lentil-shaped cautery.
And sometimes it is appropriate to cauterize additionally at the beginning of its expulsion according to this method; and those that are not thus treated, their excesses are disrupted, making them heal from these.
However, it is necessary that this be done immediately after evacuation and phlebotomy.
Chapter LVI.
On cauterization of blood flow due to accidental cutting of an artery.
If the blood flow from an artery that has been cut is due to a plague that began before the cutting, or due to the beginning of abscesses or cauterization of the limb, and similar cases, then it is difficult to remedy this.
When such a situation occurs, immediately apply your hand to the opening of the artery, and press on it with enough strength to stop the blood flow from continuing beyond your hand.
Then place the olive-coated cautery irons, both small and large, in the fire, blowing on them until they are ignited.
Then take one of those cautery irons, either small or large depending on the location and the condition in which the artery is severed, and apply it quickly after you have pressed your hand, to constrict and stop the bleeding.
And if the blood is expelled from the opening of the artery when you press your hand and apply the cautery, then take another iron immediately from those that are prepared in the fire, and continue doing this one after another until the bleeding is stopped.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is here, and make sure it does not cause other harm.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, it is not possible at those times to constrict the blood, especially when the artery is large, unless one uses one of four methods: either use cauterization as mentioned before, or perform ligation whenever it cannot be extracted because when it is extracted the extremities are burnt and the blood is constricted, or bind with a strong ligature or place over them medicines whose virtue is to restrict the blood; and they should be strengthened with a cushion and moderate constriction.
And let him who administers moderate restriction with ligatures and bandages made of cloth or by the position of burning materials and similar methods not remove them except gradually.
And if any person is to be treated and no physician or medicine is present, then gently apply the index finger on the opening of that vein as we have said, and understand the great constriction inside so that the blood remains at the upper part of the wound, and above the artery do not press; and sprinkle with cold water over the artery until the blood coagulates and thickens; and then decide whether cauterization or medicine is necessary.

And for what it is necessary to be in great caution, because in the operation which is in this chapter many times there is evacuation of blood or pus, in which the life may be lost due to the application of cautery on a vein or incision over an abscess or opening of an exit or purging of the plague or extraction of a foreign body or cutting stones and similar procedures. And with all of those things accompany fear and trembling, and in the most of their cases death.
And I warn you that this does not fall upon you in which there is danger, for some times such cases may be beyond your skills regarding the methods of many illnesses. And among them are some who become angry about their illness and are distant from understanding it, and this because of the vehemence of what they find in their illness, and due to the length of the illness, and the illness itself brings fear which signifies death.
And among them is one who gives you all his possessions and says, "I hope for health from you," but his illness is fatal.
And therefore it is not fitting that you consent to one who comes to you in this manner; and your caution should be greater than your greed and your rapacity; and do not assume any cause about it without proper knowledge which confirms what concerns you, and for which a reputable outcome follows.
In the treatment of all your patients, make a prognosis and indication of the cure to which their health is converted, for in that you will have help above all in acquiring fame and memory, praise and glory.

First Chapter.
On the treatment of water that is contained in the head of children.
Know that this illness often affects children shortly after birth when the nurse presses the child's head too hard.
And sometimes it happens for hidden causes that are not known.
But I have only seen this illness in children; and in all those whom I treated, death followed, and thus it seemed to me that there was no cure for them.
And I saw a child whose head was filled with water, and the size of their head was so greatly enlarged that they could not move because of the size of their head, and the moisture inside increased to such an extent that it led to death.
And this moisture either accumulates between the skin and the bone, or it accumulates below the bone on the skull.
For when the operation is to be performed and the moisture lies between the bone and the swelling but is only slight, it is necessary that you should cut in the middle of the head a cleft along its breadth, and let this extend about two nights' distance where the moisture flows; and this is the shape of the spatum:

And there is another method of incision, which you should consider according to where the size of the swelling and the collection of water appear, for sometimes it is more in the occipital region, or in the front part, or on the right side, or on the left; and therefore attend to the incision where the swelling and the fullness of the water appear, and do this according to what seems best to you.
And observe that you do not cut the artery and stop the flow of blood, or else the patient will suffer harm from that loss of blood and evacuation of humidity.

Chapter III.
About the extraction of arteries that are in the temples.
When someone suffers from migraines or acute headaches due to excessive moisture in the brain and similar qualities in the veins of the temples, or if such conditions are severe and do not respond to medicinal treatment, then we resort to extracting arteries in the temples or cauterizing them as we have shown.
And the method of the operation and extraction of those arteries is that the patient shaves the hair in the temples; then identify the artery visible in the temple as it is indicated by its pulse, which is hidden only in a few people due to excessive hardness.
And if it is hidden from you, then tie the head of the patient with some cloth; and afterwards rub the area with a rough cloth or evaporate the area with hot water until the artery becomes clearly visible. And now take the spatula, which has this form: And then peel the membrane from where it is attached until you reach the artery; and there remove it to the places indicated until you extract it from the membrane and reveal those of the tunics that lie beneath it, from all sides.
But if the artery is fine, now wrap it at its extremity with a ligature, and then cut off a part according to the amount by which the ends of it are lengthened and contracted, and there will be no flow of blood, for if they were not thus divided, the blood would not rise up.
And then remove one ounce of blood, or up to three ounces, from the vein.
And if the artery is large, now make sure to tie it in two places with a double thread of fine silk or with thin cords at a distance, and do not cut it before the healing of the wound, lest there be a flow of blood.
And then cut away the excess that is between two ligatures; do this at that hour or another soon after.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery to the bone or with a knife-shaped cautery until the ends of it are divided, and this will serve as a substitute for that procedure.
And it is better to perform this procedure when the patient does not have a fever or is of heated complexion; for cautery is among those causes that help in finishing off moisture, and thus it is better to do so during improvement.
Regarding the extraction of arteries, ensure that the place is clean beforehand, and let them lie on a properly prepared powder, and nearby have dry medicines ready to make the flesh grow and warm applications so that it may be healed.
And if at any other time during the procedure bleeding occurs from the artery, then you should act to constrict that blood, either by cauterization or by filling the place with cloth, and press your hand in so that the blood may be constricted. And if none of those means is available to you at that moment, then press your hand on the place until the blood is congealed, and wrap the area with water of extreme coldness until the pain subsides, and bind it as needed.
And what is more important in the extraction of an artery is that you cauterize the vein with this cautery having two cloths, after you have marked both places with a needle, and then place some combustible material, ignited, until it reaches the bone and divides the vein into two places, so that what is between the extremities may be extended, as they are not fully consolidated by this cautery.
And this is the form of the cautery:
And the sharpness of the two cloths should be noted. However, if you use less sharpness than two cloths, it is better, for if they are as sharp as two cloths, they may approach each other too quickly and cut the flesh too rapidly.
Therefore, when there is some thickness in them, the heat of the fire should be maintained and the flesh should be cut through relatively easily.
And this work is better than every other work and more concise.
And let the spaces which are between two ribs be the quantity of the thickness of a finger.
Chapter four.
About the treatment of sharp and acidic tears of the eyes.
When such acidic tears occur, and the cause is of those veins that are above the head, and if you do not find any reason for treatment in medicine, sometimes the face of the sick person turns red, and they feel a crawling sensation on their forehead as if ants were moving there, and the eyes from which these tears come appear weakened, so much so that even the hair around them can be seen clearly, and the eyelids are scorched by the acidity of the tears. The ancient practitioners performed this operation in treating such cases, and they did not consider such treatment to be without benefit.
And you command the sick person to shave the hair that is on their forehead, and then make three incisions on the forehead equidistantly according to the length of the forehead. And if the length of the incision is two fingers: one incision should be equidistant to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second according to the length a little beyond the movement of the tears which flow through the tear duct, and the third on the other side.
And be careful not to cut into the arteries that are on both sides, and extend your hand along the continuation of both jaws. And if the space between each incision is the quantity of three fingers apart.
And if you have a piece of prepared lint or dry cloth with which you squeeze out the blood.
And then insert the speculum with two sharp points, which has this shape for the incision: which is from the part of the membrane into the second incision. Remove all the tissue that is between two incisions on the crest, which is above the bone, and then proceed with this incision from the middle to the second incision. And then stretch this speculum and insert into the first incision another tool called a scalpel, sharp on one side and not sharp on the other.
And cut with the sharp side away from the adjacent flesh and towards the bone, and move it inside until you reach the middle fissure, and dissect all the vessels that descend from the head to the heels.
But continue the incision until it appears in the bone, and then proceed with this in the middle fissure to the other fissure.
And when the blood flow is equal in quantity, press the places where the collected blood is contained.

Chapter 4
On the treatment of abscesses by catharsis and the expulsion of ascending vapors from the internal parts of the head.
When there is acute and continuous bleeding from catharsis, and the humors of the patient are weakened and small, and their condition indicates debility, with the eyelids ulcerated and hair loss, and in the forehead there is sharp pain and consequent emaciation, then you should understand from these symptoms that the matter and catharsis do not come but from veins originating in the internal parts.
And the best treatment for these conditions is as follows: command the patient to shave their forehead, and then make a small incision in the middle of the forehead (beginning at the midpoint between the left temple and proceed towards the right temple; this incision should go through the bone until it exposes the bone), extending your hand from the crests of the temples which are near the mastication area.
Dry all the blood with a sponge or a dry cloth and then apply between the two edges of the incision some thickened liquid or moist cloths. And then apply above dry powder mixed with wine and oil so that it does not lead to an unhealed cauterization abscess.
And when you have done this and see that the abscess has already diminished, then scrape the bone starting where new flesh begins, and afterward treat it with a drying regimen that promotes new flesh growth. For example, take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony, mix them together, and make a plaster from it to be applied to these wounds until new flesh forms.
Chapter VI.
On the treatment of what affects the ears.
Any cause that affects the ears is one of four types: either it is a mineral stone or something similar to a stone such as iron or charcoal, or it is from a plant such as thorns or beans, or it is a fluid cause such as water and sour wine or their equivalents, or some animal.
And when a stone affects the ear or any other cause similar to those that do not adhere or clot in the ear, place the ear towards the sun, and if you see the stone, then administer some substance like violet oil or cumin, and close to it, make them sneeze with certain conditions, and plug the nose upon the occurrence of sneezing. And then place around the ear some pieces of cloth or wool, and extend the ear upwards to the sides, as movement several times helps to expel these causes.
And if it is not expelled, then administer an extraction with the instrument called a small epistome, which has the following form:
And if it is expelled with the instrument, that is good; and if not, then administer an extraction with a small piece of linen folded several times.
And if it is not expelled by any of the above methods, then make a tube of reed and insert one end of the tube into the ear canal and seal around the tube with wax and softened oil so that nothing enters except through the tube. From a foreign extract of the vein with strong attraction, for troops accustomed as we have recounted.
If it is not forgotten in this manner, now take a small lamp or prepare an apparatus with which birds are usually caught, and place that at the end of a rod, and then wrap that container decently around it. And then insert it into the ear canal with ease, and thus dry the ear of moisture.
And so if it is not forgotten with all the causes that we have recounted, now you should proceed to incise that before it becomes an abscess or spasm.
And the method of incising it is that you first puncture the malady of the cephalic vein and extract the blood according to the manner of its virtue, and then make a small cut between your fingers and turn the ear upwards, and make a slight fissure at the edge of the ear near the most prominent part. And let the fissure be in a lunar shape until it reaches the stone, and when the stone is found, remove it if you can with appropriate instruments; then close the fissure quickly at that moment, and take care of it until it heals.
And if the cause in the ear is one of the gruels that grow and become inflamed, then administer the treatment and extraction; but if you do not proceed accordingly, now take fine spatha according to this form: and administer the removal of that gruel which is lodged in the ear.
If you do not want to use water when you think that it has already been moistened by the vapor from the ear, place it in many parts, and apply extracts there with onions or fine dust, or as we have said with incision, for the extraction is easier.
In the water that has entered into the ear, it is well for the patient to use sneezing with certain preparations at the beginning, and fill his sieved ear with some old cotton, and then press on the affected ear. If this discharges the water by itself, it is good; but if not, take small pebbles or stones according to the length of a finger, heat them in the fire for a while, and have the patient insert one of these into his ear canal and jump over that part of his body with another stone hitting the stone inside the ear. Do not remove the stone until all the water has been discharged.
Do not remove the stone until all the water has been discharged. And the water will be discharged in this way: take paper or cotton, and place one end of it in the ear while holding the other end over the fire until it burns slightly.
And place another piece of cotton in it and do this as many times as necessary until all the water is discharged, and strain it with a cannula as was said about removing the stone.
In the extraction of an animal entering into that ear, if it is a small thing, handle it similarly as we have said in the division; but if it is a large object causing pain to the patient, then administer the extraction with dust and onions, and the cause of this is more serious than all other causes that occur in the ear.
In the extraction of worms generated in the ear, when you treat it with that which we have said in the division and in the tractate on distillations and do not confound your treatment, then you ought to expose the ear to the sun.
And if there appears any cause of the worms, then extract it with your finger or with fine forceps; but if no such cause appears, then take a cannula below narrow and above wide, which is of this shape: And insert the slender end into the ear according to the quantity that suffices for the patient; and then suck it out with strong suction, and do this several times until all the worms are expelled.
But if you do not obey this instruction, then apply and plug around the circumference of the cannula with wax as we have said; then administer distillations which have already proven effective in the treatment of worms, and you will find these described in the treatise on distillations.

Take what is to be inserted into the ear and heat it a little over fire. And be careful that the substance you will apply to the ear is not too irritating, for medicines for the ear cannot tolerate this.
Chapter VII.
On the treatment of blocked ears.
Sometimes infants are born from their mother's wombs with unopened ears, and this also happens in some adults, where the ear canal is blocked by infection or flesh growing inside it; and this blockage sometimes occurs at the entrance to the ear canal, which cannot be seen clearly, and sometimes it is located further out.
And when it occurs at the entrance of the ear canal, which cannot be seen clearly, it is in the most difficult positions for treatment.
And when you can see it clearly, have the patient expose their ear to the sun and observe it. And if you see a clear blockage, open it with a fine spatula, which has the following form:
If the tip of it is narrow, with one side sharp and the other not; and the spatula should be of two thin edges so as not to injure the ear.

Chapter VIII.
On the treatment of boils that occur on the eyelids.
Boils that occur on the eyelids sometimes become moist, and at other times they are dry.

Chapter IX.
Treatment for a severe condition that affects the eyelids.
Trophic areas appear on the eyelids due to some cause that resembles severity in its strength and hardness, and therefore it is called severe.

And if it is small, it will not cause harm for you because the warmth of it restores and heals it.

And after the incision, wash the wound with salt water; and treat the area with something that heals so that it may be cured.
Chapter 10.
On the treatment of injuries above the eye on the eyelid.
Injuries are often caused in the gray area that is generated in the layers of the eyelid above, and mostly affect children, worsening their eyesight due to this condition; they cannot look at the brightness of the sun because tears collect in them.

Chapter XII.
On Removing Stinging Hairs from the Eye with a Needle When the Hair is Embedded.

And if the hair is too short, now attach a long hair from among the natural hairs to it, or leave it for some time until it grows longer in place, and then lift that one.
Chapter XIII.
On the treatment of a swelling that occurs in the upper eyelid.
When a swelling appears in the eye, and it is a contraction of the eyelid when it is closed, either to the right or left side, due to poor healing of a wound, incision, or cautery, it is called leporine. This kind of swelling can be natural or accidental, caused by poor healing of wounds, incisions, or cauteries, or similar malpractice.
The method for the operation is that you should cut into that contraction and separate its edges, and place between them a piece of linen cloth, and bind it so that it may heal.
And it is not advisable to use treatments for it that dry out or constrain it, because if you do this, the swelling will return worse than before and never heal properly.
Use treatments that soften it, such as fenugreek, and boil the leaves and seeds in water or use juice from the plant, which should be dissolved in some oil. Soak a piece of cloth in this mixture and apply it for treatment.
The key to your treatment is that you should ensure it is not allowed to heal in a constricted form but rather returns to its original shape.
And this operation that we have described is simply a correction for a swelling that does not return to its original form.
Chapter XIV.

This ectropion which is done on the lower eyelid, which is properly called ectropion; and it is of some natural, others accidental.
