On the treatment of the unopened anus of a child.
Many children are born with their anuses not perforated, which must be carefully and skillfully opened. Then it should be the midwife's task to puncture that said closure with her finger; and if not, she must do so using a sharp probe, taking care not to touch the membrane.
And then apply a poultice made of boiled lint and oil; and subsequently treat that area until it is healed.
But if you fear that it may close up again, then insert a cannula for several days, and remove it when the child needs to defecate.
And it often happens that the anus becomes obstructed due to the healing of an injury or abscess; then it must be necessary to cut into that hardening, and afterwards it should be treated as we have said regarding treatment and lead.
On the treatment of fistulas that occur below.
Fistulas that occur in the lower part near the anus are usually hard and thick, which appear close to the anus or in the area nearby.
And it is one fistula or multiple.
And as that hardening becomes old and emits watery white or subtle pus, and sometimes these fistulas penetrate further.
And these penetrating ones are known by what is discharged from them during defecation and due to flatulence; and sometimes there is a bad discharge during defecation, which clearly indicates their presence.
And some of those fistulas penetrate into the space entering the bladder, and entering the urethra of the penis; and some of them penetrating into the joint of the buttock and into the root of the thigh.
And in that which is known as a penetrating fistula into the anus and a non-penetrating one, it is that you place your finger inside the anus, and insert a fine probe of hair or iron into the fistula when there is no tortuosity.
But if there is tortuosity in that one, then insert the fine probe of lead or horsehair into it until you feel the probe or hair with your finger.
And if you do not feel any of this nor proceed with the probe to find any cause of evacuation such as pus, worm, or air, as we have said, then you know that it is non-penetrating, and at that time, treat it accordingly.
And when the fistula is penetrating into the bladder or into the urethra of the penis, then the significance of that is it discharges urine from there and prohibits it from doing so to allow the affected area to heal.
But if it is penetrating into the joint of the buttock or into the bone and there is pain around the area, then the sign of that is that the probe passes through here if in the fistula there is no tortuosity leading to the bone, and there is pain around the affected area; and you will have continuous pus discharge; and medication does not help in that case, nor does it heal easily.
And in all those penetrating fistulas, there is no complete cure, and the treatment of these is laborious and futile for many physicians, upon which such certainty rests.
In that which is not penetrating and not ancient, healing by iron is hoped for as I will tell you, and this which I have experienced and proved.
And it is that the patient should lie between your thighs with their buttocks raised and their legs bent towards their stomach, and then insert a lead probe, or an iron one if it is in a tortuous fistula, until you know where the probe passes.
And if you feel the patient's pain around the anus, then it is necessary for you to insert your finger into the anus.
And if you feel with your finger that the probe has already penetrated by itself, or what you feel between your finger and that tissue or flesh, then you know certainly that it is penetrating; and at that moment do not work on it, because there is no healing in such cases, as we have said.
But perhaps some may say that it heals in some men slowly.
And for the treatment by which help is hoped for in them, you should ignite a cautery subtly according to the size of the fistula, as has been described, and insert that heated instrument into the fistula until it reaches around the anus; and then repeat this two or three times until you know that all those inflamed tissues around the fistula are now constricted like the quill of a bird.
And then treat that with bandages soaked in butter until all those constricted tissues heal, and afterwards treat it with medicines that generate and consolidate the flesh. If it heals, that is good; but if not, then it will not heal at any time by any other treatment.

On the treatment of plagues.
I have already stated in the division of the treatment of plagues what was appropriate for the book, and I will now describe the treatment of those with plague using medicines according to the regimen.
Therefore, I say that plagues are diverse depending on how the plagues are caused and depending on the location where the plague occurs.
And the causes by which such plagues are caused are things like the penetration of a stone, or the cutting by a sword or knife, or the striking with a lance, or a staff, or an arrow, or similar to those from various causes.
Here are the plagues that occur in the parts of the body such as the plague which affects the head, the neck, the feet, the belly, the back, and similar ones affecting the limbs.
And I recommend the treatment of certain plagues, so that you pause that reasoning and rule regarding other plagues.
And I will begin with the simple plagues of the head, for the memory of the compound ones will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore, I say that when you encounter a simple plague in the head and it is not a fracture, at this moment consider: if it is due to an impact from a stone or similar object, and if the bone has only been cracked but it is a large wound and you fear there might be a dangerous abscess, then immediately treat yourself and perform trepanation on the cephalic area without delay; do not postpone this unless there is some prohibitive cause for avoiding trepanation, especially if the blood that flows from the wound is minimal.
And let the trepanation be on the opposite side of the affected area; and protect the patient from swelling and rich foods and heavy meals.
But if some minor cause prohibits it, then reduce the amount of food and do not approach meat or rich foods.
And pause concerning the plague, if an abscess forms, and then apply it only to rose oil or in a mixture that is styptic.
But if you are sure of a dangerous abscess, then pause concerning the wound, if it is fresh and the blood has not yet clotted and the air has not changed the powder that I recommend to apply slightly.
And if the air has already changed that, then wait until it is healed.
And if the wound is large or caused by a sword cut and the lips of it are not suitable to be stitched together, then stitch those as I will describe regarding the stitching of such wounds.
And if the wound is already exposed to the bone and sinew, and there is no need for it to be held back but only a small suspensorium, then cut that and promptly treat the wound with medicines and restore the flesh in place of the sutures, and instruct the patient to eat foods that are easy to digest: broth and similar liquids, until they recover.
And if you find that the sutures are putrefied and do not adhere to the bone, then cut away all of it and remove it, and subsequently treat the area.
And if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which blood flow can occur and it cannot be stopped with medicines, then press on the artery. If you find that it has not been cut, then cut it with a scalpel or ligate it; and if necessity compels you to do what we have said does not suffice, then cauterize until the bleeding stops.
And if these wounds are simple and small, then the cause of them is minor. Therefore, you can powder them with this powder, as it will consolidate them before the air changes them.
And the method for the powder is as follows: take oil of sesame one part, dragon's blood two parts, and burnt frankincense three parts; mix them all together thoroughly and sieve it. Use this to fill the wound, and bind it tightly with good bandaging so that it adheres well to the wound, and let it coagulate with the blood.

On the treatment of boils and fistulas.
Know that every wound and abscess when it becomes chronic and old, and turns into an ulcer, and is not consolidated and stops discharging over time without being cut out, whether in any part of the body, is universally named a fistula, and we call this a boil.
And truly, a fistula is a feathered, hard, white node at which there is no pain but rather a sensation like the pricking of a bird's spur; and for this reason it is called a pierced boil, that is, a painful condition.
In some people who are of a moist nature, this phlegm is discharged from the arm; and in certain hours the moisture is completely removed. At certain times this moisture is moved, and it is little, and it is thick, and it is subtle.
I have shown all those causes, and the signs of them and their cure with medicines in the division.
And it is necessary for me to tell you about the treatment of fistulas, which are performed through surgical means.
And you should know that fistulas can occur in all the members of the body; among which there are fistulas that affect the large veins and arteries, or the nerves, or the ciphac (likely referring to the spinal canal), or the bowels, or the bladder, or one of the ribs, or one of the vertebrae of the back, or one of the complex joints such as those of the hand and foot; and some of these affect the vicinity of the principal member and are similar.
Therefore, of those abscesses which follow this method, it is one of the diseases difficult to heal, and does not receive a cure, and occupies both labor and ignorance.
And if there is any abscess whose depth does not reach the places mentioned above, and you hope for and trust in its cure, and wish to gain knowledge of it, then take a probe or a needle and if the abscess follows the right direction, proceed with it.
But if the abscess is tortuous, then investigate it with a probe of thin lead, for lead, due to the lightness of its body, is easily shaped and curved according to the tortuosity.
And if the abscess has openings that are frightening, it is not within your power to signify upon them with a probe.
Then irrigate the openings through those openings, for the moisture with which you irrigate will go from that one to the other openings and flow out.
And then inquire carefully according to the method by which you can know if there is bone or nerve here, and whether the depth of the abscess is far or near, or if it is an abscess whose openings are over all this within your strength, within your thorough examination, with which you thoroughly examine the disease, and the invention of the pain according to your compression on the place, and similar signs and accidental causes to the swelling.

But if the bone is exposed to you and there is corruption or blackness in it, now you should excise that until all the corruption is removed; then treat it with what will make flesh grow until it is healed.
And if it does not heal and pus continues to discharge as before, then you will know that you have not completely eradicated the corruption; at that point, uncover it again and carefully examine and clean it thoroughly with your last effort, and then restore it.
And so if you are healed, that is good; and if not, then know that that corruption is in the depth of the body, which has not been reached, and thus in that there is nothing to do but to treat that.
And if the corrupted part is small, or it is possible for you to remove that, then at that time you should remove it with fine forceps, which are suitable for this purpose.
And if some bones are involved, then carefully perform the extraction of all of them, and do not leave any of them due to your strength.
But if any cause of the humidity opposes you, and in their incision there is no fear or error, then incise those tissues or flesh where necessary, and expose the bones.

And if you encounter that, then cut the arm at the joint in the collarbone.

And similarly, when corruption spreads to the foot, then you should cut it at one of the joints.
And if it affects the entire foot, then you should amputate the whole foot. But if it spreads to the toe, then you should cut the joint at the base of the toe.
And if the corruption has already spread to the toe, then there is no remedy but dismissal, and letting the patient die.
And the method of cutting off a limb or binding it is that you tie a ligature above the part where you wish to cut, and place another ligature over the area; extend one ligature below the part, and have another extended by an assistant above; then separate the flesh which lies between two ligatures with a spatula until all the flesh is exposed; after this it can be either cut or bound.
And it is necessary to cover the area with a cloth from all sides so that it does not touch the affected site, and to ensure that the patient receives appropriate care and treatment for abscesses.

On the treatment of hemorrhoids and the quality of enemas with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in any fleshy member and the space of the abscess is extended inward so as to form a collection of pus, and it bursts and becomes perforated, and the pus is discharged from there, leaving the place empty like a vessel, and the skin that covers it remains thin like cloth, and has not reached such an extent of corruption as to affect the bone or nerves or ligaments, it is called a sinus, and is not named a fistula.
However, if the space extends inward so that the corruption affects some member, it is then called a fistula and not a sinus.
The treatment for it is to incise that skin completely as it is swollen, and especially when it becomes thin like cloth, and ensure that you do not adhere to the place because of its corruption.
However, if you hope that the skin will adhere and that the corruption has not reached an advanced stage, and there is a passage in the skin, then you should treat it with enemas.
And when you consider the sinus, if it is large and the arm with which you are treating it has a foul odor, then treat it with an Egyptian medication consisting of oil, vinegar, and raisin in equal parts, and mix them in a vessel over the fire until the medicine becomes thick like honey.
Then take the amount that is necessary and dissolve it in water and honey; use this to treat the sinus with an enema, plug the opening of the sinus, and leave the medicine inside for the duration of two hours.
Take out that which is held in disdain; and do this for so many days until the sinus is cleansed and the pus is evacuated.
And sometimes it is given as an enema with water of ashes, when that medicine is not at hand; and you should take the ash of wheat, put it over the water; and then strain that, and give that as an enema until you are confident that the sinus is now closed.
And if the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the water of ashes, then give an enema with water and honey mixed thoroughly three times, and give another enema with vinegar and honey mixed, for the property of honey is to cleanse and wash, and the vinegar causes the sinus to adhere, especially when the vinegar has a preponderance of astringency and dryness.
And when you do this for several times and are confident that the sinus is now cleansed, then give an enema with water that promotes flesh growth.
And you should take of the decoction of dyafinicon and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent vinegar; or give an enema with several medicines and other decoctions that have the properties which I have already written about in the chapter on decoctions.
And if the orifice of the sinus is narrow such that the enema tube cannot be received, then you should gradually enlarge it with a small instrument, and pause in the dilation wrapped in Egyptian medicine or basilicon decoction until it is enlarged.
And similarly, if the orifice is too large, then you should sew together the edges with a suture and leave it in such a quantity that the enema can be introduced without additional addition.
And if the orifice of the one through which the arm drains is raised to the upper parts, then dry that in the pus-filled place that is there where the arm passes underneath, for when the arm is compressed at the depth of the bend the flesh will be generated there.
And if it is not possible to dry the sinus underneath in the vicinity of the pus as you wish, then perforate so that you prepare the member with preparation by which the arm drains lightly as you are equipped and so that nothing in it impedes the arm.
But that which is over the sinus opening, which you must change at all times so that it dries what is adhering to the arm. And so that you may know whether what drains from the arm is much or little, or is digested, or is undigested.
And with this, you should consider the location of that sinus, whether the patient feels pain there or not, and whether there is an abscess there.
And when your treatment is prolonged in this manner, and you see that the material which is placed over the sinus opening has adhered or is about to adhere, then on the third day you loosen it, and when you loosen the dressing, then change the cloth and renew the application.
But if the sinus has become empty of subtle pus, then do not despair of its adherence, for its healing is imminent, since some people take a long time to generate flesh in their dressings, and others are the opposite.
And if from the sinus there has been evacuated for many days thick undigested matter, then you know that the sinus will not adhere; and if the adherence of the sinus is delayed and becomes prolonged, then adjust to the drying properties of the medicines, and if the medicines are moist in their substance and in their virtue, they should be dried as well when applied, adjusting for any excess addition of grease.

On the treatment of paronychia and worn nails, and on cutting an added finger joint and sectioning.
Paronychia is an abscess that arises from under the nail of a toe or finger of the hand or foot, and it can also occur in other fingers. When it becomes prolonged and its treatment is delayed, it can become an abscess of an already inflamed area, or it may corrupt, causing pus to ooze inside which corrodes the root of the nail. It often corrupts the entire nail, and sometimes spreads to the bone inside, producing a foul odor, and the extremity of the finger or toe becomes blackened, and its color turns dark.
And when you have done what we have said in the division and do not see improvement in your treatment, then it is necessary that you incise with a scalpel all the superfluous tissue that is rotten from the nail and promptly cauterize the wound thereafter, for prompt cautery is helpful in such cases.
But if the bone is sound and the nail is healthy, and the addictions of the nail have already compressed the flesh inwardly, causing pain, then it is necessary that you apply a fine probe under the angle of the nail where the flesh hurts, and push it up and incise that flesh with ease, and place over what remains of the flesh corrosive medicines until it is completely removed; and thereafter treat it with ointments until it is healed.
If the corruption has already penetrated the bone, now is the time for you to incise the bone and extract it, because there is never a cure when the bone is corrupted.
And if the corrosion and corruption spread to the finger, now you should incise it at one of the joints as is prescribed in its treatment, and then cure it until it is healed.
However, if there is an injury such as a puncture or bruise to the nail, and there is severe pain in that area, you should initially bleed the patient at the beginning, and then dry the nail with a sharp instrument in a transverse section from above downward, but be careful not to cut into the flesh below the nail, for this would cause the patient severe pain and lead to the formation of excess scar tissue in that location; then treat that area with something that relieves pain.
However, in the case of a finger that is swollen, which often occurs in many people, it is mostly filled with flesh, and there is little bone or nail within.
And some of them arise at the root of the joint, or the swelling is in some joints of the fingers. However, what arises in some joints of the finger is not serious, whereas what arises at the joints is more serious.
And for those that are fleshly swellings, the incision is shallow, and you should cut it behind its root with a broad instrument.
However, for what arises at the root of the joint, treatment is difficult; therefore, be careful not to amputate it.

On the extraction of the vein of the leg.
This vein is generated in the thighs in warm lands such as Al-Ategi in the land of Arabia and in hot, dry lands of India where no grass or crops grow.
And this vein is generated in other parts of the body besides the thighs.
And its formation is from putrefaction that occurs below the skin, just as worms and vermin come from within the body, generating between the skin and the flesh.
And the sign of the occurrence of this vein is that inflammation happens suddenly in the thigh; and near the area, blisters appear; and soon the vein begins to emerge from the place of those blisters, resembling a plant sprout.
And when the end of it appears, you should then wrap around it a piece of lead weighing one dram to two drams, and attach that area and let the lead hang from the thigh.
And so many times when you find some cause in the parts outside, wrap that with lead and wool. And if a wound is long, then you cut one part of it and wrap the remainder; and do not cut that part too deeply until it has healed outwardly, for if you cut too deeply, it contracts inwardly into the flesh and causes an abscess and putrefaction in the place and a bad ulceration.
For it must be treated gently and extracted little by little until it is completely removed from the rear of it and there remains no cause for it in the body.
And this vein is found in some people extending to a length of five palms and I have heard that it has reached up to twenty palms in length.
And if it is cut during the time of its treatment, then insert the reed into the tract of it and pierce that one along the length of the body until everything that is in the matter has been evacuated, and treat the area with putrefaction for several days with medicines; and subsequently cure that one according to the treatment of abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has multiple openings, especially when it appears at the junction of the foot or on the foot, and it comes out through multiple openings, and is branched from each opening.
And therefore treat that one as we have said in the division and according to what has been mentioned above.
Regarding the section on worms generated under the skin and it is called bovine illness.
This illness is called in some lands bovine illness because often among them there is a worm of which one is born between the skin and the flesh, and it moves throughout the body up and down, and it is shown by sensation beneath the skin from member to member until it breaks through the skin and comes out.
Regarding the generation of worms is through the putrefaction of certain humors, just as it happens with worms and serpents and worms and grubs in the belly.
It is feared because of its malice, for when it enters the body and rises to the head, and reaches the heart, immediately it burns strongly within and penetrates and destroys the heart, causing multiple dangers.
And therefore when you want to cure that and extract it, then it is not done except by observing the movement and appearance of it to the sensation, and then you should press what is above it and what is below it tightly with bandages and constriction, and soon it dries up and extracts that.
And if it penetrates into the flesh and you do not find it, then you should pause to cauterize with fire over the area where it resides, and the main cause that is feared is corruption of it by which it corrupts the area as we have said.

On the treatment of nasal afflictions.
This illness is called "nafir" in our land, and it is a pain that occurs in certain members of the body, and soon changes from one member to another.
And I have already seen that as I will now recount to you. I went to visit a sick woman in a village and uncovered her arm, where I found a small inflammation in the vena cephalica of the arm. After rinsing it for about an hour, I saw that this inflammation was moving just as a worm moves upwards, but more quickly than one would expect, like live silver flowing from place to place.
Then the pain moved from that location to the muscle, and it stayed there for about an hour.
And then it moved to the other side of her body until it reached the other arm.
And then she told me that it was moving throughout her entire body as I observed; and I was very amazed by its movement and the speed with which it moved from one limb to another. And I had seen something like this before in my own patients in that manner, but I have never seen pain being transferred from one limb to another so distinctly. But I did not see this in my patients as I did in the woman.
And I did not think this was possible except that the woman was of an elderly age, with weak limbs and exposed veins, and in her condition, this airiness or lightness could be perceived to move.
And it is necessary that this does not appear according to this retrograde movement in those who are robust and have healthy limbs with veins preserved.
And when you want to treat it and the patient feels that pain, immediately apply pressure on that area and let it move quickly until that restricted airiness has passed, and then cauterize the location.
If you do not use it to wash your hands, then treat that with an excursion of the body and with what expels windiness, and apply those against stinking pills and against pills of serape and similar.
Of the extraction of splinters.
Splinters are varied according to their types and the parts of the body where they lodge, for their variety is such that some have large points, others small ones, concave or blunt tips; some are solid. There are those with three angles, and there are those that resemble tongues; and there are those that are like slender sticks.
And the signs of a splinter in the brain are when it lodges in such a way that it penetrates the bone and emerges on the cephalic side, which is above the brain, caused by violent cold, scotoma, vertigo, rigidity in the hands, inflammation and burning of the tongue, spasms, confusion of understanding, vomiting of bile, and often speech is lost and the voice destroyed. Humid white matter similar to powder exits from the affected area, and the appearance shows swelling of the flesh.
Therefore, if I show you these signs, then you should refrain from treatment and the extraction of the arrow, unless it is outside.
Mark the signs of the arrow when it hits the heart, so that it is near the left nipple, and feel there whether it is fixed in hard tissue, not soft; and light the movement of the arrow resembles the pulse movement, and causes black blood to flow, and chills those parts, and cold sweat breaks out, and fainting occurs; therefore you will know that death descends soon.
And the signs when the arrow has struck the lung are frothy blood from the wound, and the veins near the neck are swollen, and the complexion of the patient changes, and he breathes out great sighs, and seeks to draw in cold air.
And if the arrow hits the membrane that is in the thighs, and the pain then is near the small ribs, and it causes great and violent pain and sighing, and there is no strength left in all the limbs of the muscles.
And if the arrow hits the stomach, then very little of the food in the wound is digested.
And if the arrow hits the abdomen, and it is lodged there, with some part of the wound torn, whether it be muscle, membrane, or bowel, which is ruptured, then there is no hope for recovery from the extraction of the arrow.
And if the arrow hits the bladder, and urine comes out from it, and some part of it extends outward, and there is pain over the patient, then you will know that it is fatal.
Like other members also as in the face, the neck, and the throat, and the shoulders and the helpers, and the vertebrae of the back, and the collarbone, and the rib and things similar to these members, they are saved according to the major dispositions when the arrow does not find a vein or artery and is not a venomous arrow.
And I will recount this to you about the nature of these arrows, so that you may understand thereby about their treatment.
The reason for this is that when an arrow was embedded in the lacrimal bone of his eye at the root of the nose, I extracted it from him on the other side, left the granules behind in the ear canal, and he healed without any harm coming to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which had embedded in his eye within the canal, leaving the eyelid intact. The arrow was already concealed and only a small part of it could be reached to which the shaft adhered. It was a large sharp arrow of those used by Turkish warriors, with barbs but no grooves.
And the Jew recovered, and no harmful incident occurred to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian man who had it embedded in his throat, and it was an Arabic arrow, which had barbs; then I incised around that between two guidelines, and it was already submerged in his throat; then I carefully removed it, and the Christian man recovered.
And I extracted another arrow from a man who had it embedded in his abdomen, and he was thought to die because of it; but after thirty days had passed and there was no change in his condition, I incised around the arrow and removed it, and he recovered without any harmful incident.
And there was a man in whom an arrow had been embedded at the base of his neck, and the injury had healed around it, but after seven years the arrow came out from the root of the collarbone.
And there was a woman in whom an arrow had struck her abdomen, and the injury had healed internally around it, and she did not experience any change in her condition, nor did she suffer any difficulties with her natural functions.
And there was another man in whom an arrow struck his face, and the injury healed, but the arrow remained embedded without causing any pain, and similar cases are known.
And the arrow was extracted from the man who was the prince of the king; it had struck near the midpoint of his nose and had slightly deviated to the right, and the entire arrow was hidden inside.
And I was called to treat him after the arrow had been embedded in him for about three days.
And then I found the wound of the arrow quite narrow; and then I examined it carefully, but did not feel any sensation there; however, he felt a pain below his right ear, and thus I suspected that this pain was due to the tip of the arrow.
And I applied a plaster to the area that had drawing and digestive properties, believing that it would cause the area to swell so that the location of the arrowhead would become apparent.
And no significant reaction occurred in the area where I believed the arrowhead to be; and I continued to apply the plaster for several days without any incident; meanwhile, the wound began to heal, and after about three days, he felt the arrow inside his nose and informed me of this.

Then I adjusted the opening of the sore with that acute medicine until the tip of the arrowhead became visible to me, and it took me time for this over a period of four months.

And some doctors judged that the cartilage of the nose could not be restored, but I did restore it, and the sore healed, and the patient was completely healthy without any further cause for concern.

On phlebotomy of the veins.
The veins for phlebotomy of which one usually performs bloodletting, there are thirty, of which sixteen are in the head. Of these, two pulsating veins, which are near the ears, called alhasisain; two branches that appear on each temple; and two veins that are in the two tear ducts by the eyes, which are called aspicientz; and the vein prepared in the middle of the forehead, and the vein at the tip of the nose; and two tendons which are near the neck; and two veins that are in the upper lip; and two veins that are in the lower lip; and these are the veins that are called algelic; and there are two veins below the tongue. The veins that are phlebotomized in the arms are five. Of these, one is called cephalic, and it resembles the wild thyme; and people call it "the vein of the head," and it is also called sorda, and it is a median vein, composed of a branch from the basilic vein, and another branch from the cephalic vein; and they call this one among the common people "vein of the whole body"; and basilica is the vein that lies on the domestic side, and it is called "little finger," and the common people name this "abdominal vein." And there are tendons in the arm, and it lies over the humerus, and this is where bloodletting is performed, and it appears prominently on the forearm; and salvatella is the vein that lies between the thumb and the little finger, and these are in both sides.
And in the leg and the thigh there are three veins. One of these is behind the curvature of the wild thyme side; and the second is sophena, and its location is behind the domestic thigh; and the sciatic vein, and its location is behind the wild thigh; and in the thigh there are other veins similar to those.
The treatment of veins near the ears is for catarrhs, emigrations, and old ulcers of the head.
And the quality of opening those veins is as I recommend to you: shave the patient's head and rub it well at the occipital region where the two veins are; press down on the patient’s neck with a bandage so that the two veins appear near each ear in two locations lower on the head; and feel for them with your finger, and if you can sense the pulse of those veins with your finger, here is when to proceed.
Then take the lancet, and use that which is sharp, and then insert it below the vein along the groove until the blood reaches the bone. And then raise your hand with the vein and its groove upwards and cut the vein with a sharp incision. And the length of the incision should be about two fingers' width or slightly more; and let as much blood out as you wish.
And then apply pressure with a pad and leave it there until it is healed.
And already at the beginning of the book, I have described the incision and cauterization of those veins.
But for the two arteries near the temples, the treatment of phlebotomy is for old and persistent migraines, acute suppurating conditions in the eyes, and severe accumulations of excess fluids around the eyes.
And the quality of phlebotomy of those arteries is as I recommend to you: have the patient press their neck with force until the two veins become visible clearly, and demonstrate the pulse of those veins with your finger; then you may proceed.
Raise the vein above the ligature to the parts above with your finger, and insert the phlebotomy knife below, raising the vein up; and cut it as you did for the two other veins; let the blood flow according to the quantity of your need.
Then loosen the compression on the patient, and place your finger on the vein for one hour, then place it over that cut and apply pressure with the palm and fingers firmly around it, and leave it until it is healed.
And the reminder of them has been mentioned before, and their incision and attraction have been described at the beginning of the book.
And thus, the procedure of phlebotomy of the frontal vein is similar to the cephalic vein phlebotomy for ancient illnesses of the face and for migraines, rigors, and foul-smelling ulcers.
And the quality of the phlebotomy is as I recommend to you; that is, the patient should hold their arm with a cloth until the vein appears; then take the instrument called a trocar, which has this shape:
Place the sharp point of the trocar at the head of the instrument over that vein and press it down on it either tilted or for some other reason as described, and let the blood flow according to the quantity you desire; then loosen the puncture of the patient and hold it until it is healed.

Of the quality of phlebotomy it is that the patient plunges his arm into hot water and performs friction thereon until the veins become visible; and he places a cord over the joint of the arm, and the place of the vein appears above the said joints, and from which those branches extend across the face of the arm in various directions.
And so the phlebotomy should be performed on the largest branch below the elbow near the wrist, for it is better and more effective.
And if you perform phlebotomy on that area of the arm's surface, then be careful of the nerves which are below it on the arm's surface, and stop your phlebotomy when you reach a point where you would otherwise cut across, as if you were trying to divide it.
And so the phlebotomy is finished.
But if the letting of blood is prohibited; then you should immerse the arm in hot water again and allow the blood to flow in that area until it is evacuated.
And if the phlebotomist errs on the first attempt, then repeat the phlebotomy above it slightly, for that location is effective; and do not fear this mistake, but be careful of the nerves as we have said.
And similarly do the phlebotomy of the other arm in the same manner.
And for the sciatic vein, the location is as we have said, below the thigh behind the knee.
And the benefit of phlebotomy is to alleviate pain in the side when one is suffering from excessive blood.
Of the quality of the phlebotomy is that the patient enter into a bath, and hasten and strain the body from the width between the thighs up to above the heel in four places with a long slender bandage, for it appears only by this.
And when it appears, then you phlebotomize that according to each angle as is possible and according to the transverse, which is better; or incise that by incision or section it by cutting, for the place of it is salt.
And it is in the words of a man slightly weakened; and if you find that, and it does not appear to the sense of all, then phlebotomize some branch of it, and that is the one which appears between the two of the hand behind the ring finger and the little finger.
And be careful of the nerves, and let the amount of blood flow that you wish.
And then loosen the bandage and pause over the place of the phlebotomy to strain the place, for it is quickly healed.
Of venotomy and of the qualities of using those.
Venotomies are usually from horns, or arteries, or veins.
And venotomy is done in two ways: one of them is as incision and extraction of blood, and the other is venotomy without incision.
And this venotomy that is without incision is in two ways, for it can be with fire or without fire.
And for the venotomies that are administered with incision and extraction of blood there are fourteen locations on the body, of which one is where a venotomy is usually paused at the back of the head; and another middle location is at both sides of the neck; and venotomies of the chin below the jaw beside the mouth; and venotomies of the two cheeks; and venotomies of alohoster, which is above the earlobe; and venotomies of the two forearms; and venotomies of the two thighs; and venotomies of the two feet.
Of bloodletting we take the blood not from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and thereby we do not capture the virtue through those, just as it happens with phlebotomy; nor is it necessary for bloodletting to be done near a fire or without a fire in any disease caused by retention until the entire body is evacuated.
If necessity prompts bloodletting due to illness or continuously, administer that at any time at the beginning of the month and at the end of it, and in the middle of it and at whatever interval may be appropriate.
And when you encounter any of those causes, and especially if it is in the third or middle of the month, I command that bloodletting be performed after two or three hours have passed from midday.
And the benefit of bloodletting from the neck is that when it relieves heaviness in the head and also what has settled in the eyes; but it should be done after the evacuation of the entire body.
And this vein is in the place of phlebotomy of the cephalic region.
And it is not good to use that which is of full friction, or in which there is a cold, because it gives great harm to the patient.
And for this reason, it is not advisable to use those veins, and in the heads of which there are usually fresh illnesses.
But for the one who continues above that, forgetfulness is caused by it; and for this reason, you should command the vein-bleeder to pause his hand a little bit behind, fearing the generation of forgetfulness.
But phlebotomy of the temporal region is in the place of venesection of the dark vein and of the basilic vein: and for this it is beneficial for asthma, and for constriction of breath, and for rupture of the breathing organ, and for coughs, and for excess.
And it is necessary to raise the temporal vein a little, for if it is lowered too much, it generates weakness in the body and in the stomach.
And the phlebotomy of the occipital region is beneficial for accidental pains in the head and for ophthalmia, headache, coryza, and pains in the roots of the teeth; and it is in the place of venesection of the basilic vein.
And it is necessary to command the vein-bleeder not to press his hand near the incision so that he does not injure the artery and cause a flow of blood.
And phlebotomy below the chin is beneficial for alcohol in the mouth, and for corruption of the gums, and for symptoms similar to those illnesses that are in the mouth, and it is in the place of venesection of the alheric vein which is on both lips.
Avoid the windiness that is made between the two spatulas by applying a bandage, which is made by filling it with heat.

Avoid windlasses on minor areas carefully and distinctly, for they draw blood from the entire body, and apply them to old pains in the loins and back, and to the bladder; and they induce menstruation, and apply them to boils and abscesses; and they serve as a substitute for phlebotomy of two saphenas, the alahorcoben, and two cavilhas.
However, they weaken the entire body and can cause fainting in some people.

Of the quality of the position of the cupping vessels is that you place the vessel at the beginning empty and suck it moderately, and do not prolong the position of the cupping vessels, but pause them gently, and remove them cautiously, so that the humors come to the place by occurrence equal. And do not cease to repeat this and continue until you see the place already red and swollen, and the hardness of the blood appears.
And now you should incise and repeat the suction gradually, and then observe the disposition of the body.
For the person who has tender flesh with few pores should be incised once and not pressed, so that the area is not ulcerated.
And instruct the cupper to enlarge the incision slightly and to prolong it a bit, and to moderate the suction with ease.
And if the blood is thick, now you should incise twice, for in the first incision the way of subtlety to the blood and to its moisture is opened. But in the second for the acquired scrutiny of extracting the thick blood.
And if the blood is muddy, now make a third incision so that it achieves the desired effect.
And finally, when we want to extract the blood gradually or in small amounts, one incision is sufficient.
And if we want to extract a large amount of blood, we make a larger incision; and if we know that the blood is thick, now you should make a prolonged incision.
Regarding the term of incision, in which the depth of the wound is concerned alone.
And if the one whose wound is thick, hard, dark, and has narrow pores, it is necessary that the places for cupping be anointed with soothing, opening, and resolving oils; if it is in summer time, such as oil of aloes or oil of violets, or oil of rose petals, or oil of chicken grease; and if it is in winter time, then oil of narcissus, or oil of lilies, or oil of chamomile, or blood, and similar things.
And if the excrement is thick and foul, then the oil should be of henna, or balsam oil, or aloes oil, or anise oil, and similar things.

And for the one whose blood is of thick humidity, wash their cupping sites with vinegar water, or with water of myrtle and sumac, and similar things.
And for the one whose excretions are thick, wash their cupping sites with aged vinegar, or with water of savory, which is marjoram, or of anise decoction, or chamomile, and similar things.
And it is necessary that cupping be administered before bathing; after which it should be given immediately after the bath by an hour or two, and it is necessary for them to rest near the location of the cupping.
What is needed so that bleeding may be regulated either by cupping or phlebotomy before bleeding.

And this is sought not for any other reason but for the attraction of blood from a limb, just as we apply a cupping vessel to the breast in the disease of excessive flow of blood from the nose; and they use it so that the coagulated inflammation of the limb may be dissolved, just as when applied to the abdomen and navel; for this rarefies the part and heats it, thereby alleviating pain by resolving the flatulence.
And it is applied to the sinuses when they are blocked or weak, for by the power of attraction it raises up obstructions and eradicates weakness from its place.
And similarly when it is applied to the liver and spleen to expel flatulence, for in these it confines the air.
And these cupping vessels are administered only after they have been heated with suction; and some are applied over fire.

Form of the cupping vessel administered over fire: the diameter of its opening should be two fingers wide, as we have formed it; and the quantity of the pre-on should be half a palm; and at its center, in the place that is in the circuit, there should be half of it, the thickness should be small according to the amount that fits inside, like a needle.
It should be made of earthenware or citrus wood, with thick edges, leather, cushions, and smooth so that it does not hurt the limb due to its position on it.
And let a thread be passed across the bone or iron, upon which a candle is placed near the fire.
And this large suction cup should be larger than the one we have recommended; and its size should be adjusted according to the illness and the condition of the person using it.
And the suction cups for children and those who are weak should be different from those used by young and robust individuals.

And when it is placed on the patient's limb, and the hand is on the area we have mentioned, then when we have the suction cups and do not need to hold the hand so that the vapor rises over that area, the suction cup will dissolve immediately.

Chapter xcviij.
Bloodletting should not be administered in movable parts but in those members where it is impossible to apply cupping due to their small size, such as the gums and palate and similar areas; or in bare flesh, such as the fingers and nose and similar areas.
And the quality of using those leeches that you have found in the clear waters near decaying matter.
And then they should be left in clear water day and night until they are hungry, so that no remaining food stays in their stomachs. And then evacuate the body at the beginning of bloodletting and cupping. And then rub the patient's limb until it reddens. And then place them on the affected area.
And when they are full and satisfied.
And if suction with cupping is possible in the area, it is better to proceed with it.
And if not, then wash the area with cold water and then with lukewarm water; and it should be rubbed and pressed.
And if the blood extends beyond the bite marks of the leeches and there is excess bleeding, then place a cloth in cold water and apply it until the bleeding stops.
And if the bleeding continues, then sprinkle powdered vitriol or other astringent powders on it until the bleeding stops; or apply crushed parched fava beans to the area and leave them until they adhere to the skin, at which point the bleeding will stop.
In case it is necessary, repeat the application of leeches, when it is possible to change those.
And if the leeches are prohibited from attaching, then at that time wash the area with fresh blood or place a needle in the spots to let blood flow; and afterwards they should be paused, because those when feeling any blood will immediately attach.
And when you want them to stop, sprinkle a little powdered aloes, or salt, or ash on them, and they will cease immediately.
Thus begins the chapter of the third part: on restoration. This chapter is particularly necessary in the art of medicine; it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations of bones.
Oh, son, be aware that the foolish doctors and the common folk boast about this chapter, as well as those who for any length of time have not regarded the books of the Ancients nor read a single letter from them.
And for this reason it is done that this method of the scientists in our land is private; and I have not forgotten any of it even for a moment; and I have not adopted what follows, except through the length of reading the books of the Ancients and studying to understand them, from which I extracted the science.
And then I directed myself to experimentation and study throughout the duration of my life.
And I have already described in this chapter all that my science contains and on which my experience is based; now I condense it for you and release it from the burden of lengthiness; I abbreviate it to the point of brevity; I demonstrate it at the end of clarity; and I present many forms of instruments used therein, as added by explanation, just as I did in the two preceding chapters.
First chapter.
A summary and collection of fractures of the bones, from which a preface is necessary before we begin the recollection of the parts of fractured members and their various treatments. Therefore, let us proceed with the prologue of this chapter to summarize the discourse; and let the chapters be such that at the beginning they force the artisans and practitioners to stand on the truth of these matters; so that you and those like you who wish to know or learn this noble art may do so quickly.
And therefore I say that when it happens to someone that they suffer a fracture, dislocation, torsion, or fall, at that moment it is necessary for you to consider whether to perform phlebotomy or to bandage them, or both together, if no cause prohibits this, such as weakness of constitution, or the person who has been affected being an infant, elderly and decrepit, or during times of extreme heat or cold.
And let him be content with food that is light, such as fresh herbs and the meat of birds and small animals; we prohibit fatty meats and filling foods, including any food that fills the veins with blood, until he is assured of a safe resolution of abscesses, and unless he fears an effusion of matter into the limb.
And now return the patient to his original regimen, which he was accustomed to before the injury.
And when the broken bone begins to be restored, at this point it is necessary for the patient to be fed with nutritious foods that are moist and heavy, in which there is viscosity, such as foods made from boiled wheat with water, rice, peas, beef stomachs, eggs, fresh meat, and similar items. With this regimen, coagulation of the fracture occurs more easily and better.
And know that bones broken in adults, as well as in infants and the elderly, cannot be set or consolidated according to their nature by drying of the limbs.
But nature adjusts and allows you to place on the broken bone from every part some cause similar to glue, in which there is thickness that adheres and strengthens it as they are conjoined together, until they come to an end of strength and firmness, just as fire at the beginning, from no cause prohibiting it from its works.
And for this reason it is necessary that the patient abstains from foods that are heavy and viscous and coarse, as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of the limbs, for a fracture of the bone of the thigh is different from a fracture of the bone of the head; and a fracture of the bone of the foot is different from a fracture of the bone of the arm; and similarly all the other limbs are different among themselves.
And you will find a description of every kind of fracture, explained in its own separate chapter.
Describe the diversity of fractures within themselves. And the types of bone fractures are diverse in themselves; for one type of arch-shaped fracture is different from those that occur in the stars; and some fractures depend on the length of the bone: thus there are fractures of the shaft, splinters, and complete and incomplete fractures, just as when a pen is prepared to write, there is a fracture due to pressure and disruption in the head, and there is a small fissure fracture.
And each type has its own proper treatment in the restoration of it, which will be recalled separately in their respective places.
Describe the signs of a bone fracture. And by this a bone fracture is known: there is a deformity of the limb, and an abnormal prominence and appearance of it to the touch, as well as pain when you press on it with your hand.
And therefore when there is no apparent deformity or pain in the area where you touch the bone and move it, and the patient does not feel much pain, then there is no fracture here.
However, it is possible that there may be a deformity or small fissure. Therefore, do not neglect to press on it with your hand and by compressing it entirely; wait for the medicine from among those which will be recalled shortly, which is appropriate at the moment when you consider it necessary for the location; and it may be tight due to slight constriction.
And know that when the place is arched and not divided into two or another part from where it did not occur in the swelling, you must begin your work on the correction of the affected area where the abscess has occurred.
And if an abscess occurs there, then leave it for some days until the abscess subsides; and afterward bandage it according to each method that you can by lightness and ingenuity.
And know that the restoration of it and the alignment of it is easier than with the bone in which the swelling has occurred.
And afterward strengthen it according to what will come to mind regarding it.
But if the swelling is in the bone, then it is necessary to extend this broken limb from two parts, whether it is the hand or the foot; or use your hand if the member is small; or use two cords or one cord and your hand.
And place yourself in such a position that you can put the limb in a natural and proper position, while the parts of the broken member are extended, then it is necessary for you to return those addictions with skill and according to each method that you can back to their places.
And be careful according to your strength that no harm comes from your operation either by mistake or injury; and strive according to your ability that you apply one bone to its companion bone according to their best forms.
And it is necessary that at that opportune moment you touch with your hand that which needs attention, and if you see any differing cause, correct it and bandage it according to the extent of your skill.
And from the improper stretching and compression, just as false doctors do with their repeated manipulations, they cause an abscess to occur because of their operation, or they cause contraction in the limb just as we have seen this happen due to their repeated manipulations.
And the quality of the restriction of the tranquil limb is what I recommend; for you should know that broken limbs vary in their smallness and magnitude and in the form of those fractures.
And for those that are small, such as the arm and the fingers and similar parts, then the wrapping should be of soft, thin cloth.
But for those that are large, such as the thigh or the chest, it is necessary to use a firm bandage, because the tough wrapping connects and secures the large limb evenly in place, thereby preventing any injury.
And after beginning, after your initial treatment for the fracture, pause conveniently for this, over the place of the fracture with three or four bandages as the limb requires; and apply your hand gently to the bandage.
And apply to it the best-placed poultice at the part above the place of the fracture, and tie a bandage so as not to hinder the swelling with which you have bound the place of the fracture. And then extend the wrapping around the place of the fracture a little, and gradually loosen the bandage until you have removed most of it.
And then take another bandage and wrap it around the fractured area with the previous wrapping, and then apply the poultices below the fracture; and let your operation be in tightening the bandages and loosening them as we have said about the first wrapping above.
And then pause between the wrappings with a piece of linen or cloth on which the curvature of the fracture is evened out, if there is any curvature; and if not, do not pause for any reason.
And then pause on the other bandage, and then apply plasters properly made immediately if there is no inflammation or abscess in the area, and at that time place on what causes the abscess and removes the inflammation; and leave this for some days; and then you tie on the plasters.
And let the plasters be about half a cubit wide and well made; or they may be of willow bark; or of palm leaves; and let the width of each plaster be according to this form and this figure:
However, the plaster that is placed over the fracture should be a bit thicker and wider than the others, and the length of the plaster according to how it fits the limb in size; then bind over the plasters with another bandage as per your first binding; and tie above this with fine linen as we have said about binding, and it should be that your binding is tighter near the location of the fracture, and the further away from the fracture, the looser the binding.
And let the threads be medium in thickness and fineness, and let them be soft; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen some foolish people use ropes made of animal intestines for their sutures, it is a great mistake, when you see that the tension is unequal.
And similarly, fine threads are not suitable either, for you will not achieve the desired tension with them, and it is necessary that there be more than a finger's width between stitch and stitch.
And if the patient is in pain at the ends of the threads near the sutures in those places, then pause and use silk or carded wool between them so that it does not cause harm.
In some cases, a fracture is caused by trauma, and disruption occurs in the bone, and I will come to the memory of this in the second chapter dedicated to such particularities.
And know that not all broken limbs should be set with sutures on the first day; the reason for this is that when the limb is large, it does not need to be placed in bandages immediately but after about five or seven days or more, according to your certainty of the onset of inflammation.
And this is the method of plasters used by the Ancients to treat fractures, dislocations, and twists, which were applied over such injuries.
Method of making a plaster for the rapid restoration of fractures, suitable for various conditions and especially for women and children, as it does not cause heat or irritation: take powdered flour, which is the fine powder that settles on the walls of the mill from the movement of the millstones.
And confine it as it is, but sift it with white wool, and pause the compounding of it not thin nor subtle; and then use it.
Another method for plasters to heal fractures, dislocations, and twists: recipe of asafetida, of litharge, of acacia, of eryngo root, and migraach, which is the rind of wild millet, of each one drachm; of myrrh and aloes one half drachm each; colocynth two drachms; all should be thrice sifted and mixed with water or with white wool if the composition is heated; and then use this plaster, for it is well composed and quickly restores broken bones, and applies to many men.
Another method for a plaster to treat fractures and twists: recipe of migraach and the bark of white nettle one drachm each; of myrrh and aloes one half drachm each; of acacia six drachms; of Armenian bole two drachms.
All should be thrice triturated in a mortar with a crushing motion, and then sieved, and compounded with water or white wool, and it should be administered.
Method for a plaster for joints and bones that have come out of their places, and for accidental pain in those areas and for fissures and fractures: take pure wool and immerse it in sour wine and warm oil and apply it to the location.
In this plaster, there is no virtue of restoration, but it is good for mature abscesses and properly expels pains.
Another method for a plaster that restores a broken bone: take leaves of the wild fig tree and leaves of wild poppy; crush them all and make a plaster from them.
Method of applying a paste for the treatment during the restoration of a bone fracture and when you wish to resolve the remnant of an abscess: take equal parts of plantain, chamomile flowers, violets, and spelt flour; mix all together and apply with water if the limb is not inflamed.
If the limb is inflamed, now prepare it with colander water that has been moistened or with water and use it.
Another method of making a stronger paste for resolving an abscess that is harder than the first one, which occurs during the restoration of a limb: take equal parts of plantain leaves and linseed seeds, hawthorn, clover, marjoram, violet flowers, and chamomile; mix all together and apply with sage water or warm water or with vinegar, depending on the condition of the limb and according to its color.
However, Hippocrates does not mention in his book that a paste should be applied to the broken limb during its restoration but rather wax made of beeswax and oil, and no other cause; he recommends that it should be midway between thickness and fineness.
However, Galen is seen to have applied pastes to the broken limb during its restoration and for causes such as slight drying with heat, such as myrrh, onions, and similar to those causes that we have mentioned.
Method of making a paste to apply for injury and pain: take equal parts of egg whites, linseed, human hair cut or bird feathers, plantain, and salt; mix all together and sieve them, then make into a paste.
The amount according to which it is necessary that the bandage remain on and when it should be loosened, is such that you consider whether pain does not occur or improvement does not take place nor is the bone dislocated from its place; therefore, do not loosen it for several days. And if severe pain or improvement or inflammation occurs at the site, then you should be cautious or loosen that bandage immediately, and remove the plaster.
And take a piece of linen and a sponge soaked in seawater, and immerse it in cold water; with this wash the area until the inflammation subsides and the pain is alleviated; then let the limb rest for an hour; afterwards, place it on wool saturated in vinegar and oil or rose oil, and bandage it for one night until you are sure that no abscess has formed, and to allow the swelling of the limb to subside, and to ensure that the pain is reduced.
And then apply that gentle bandaging and small plasters without tightening it as much as the first time, and gently do this until it is healed.
And if you see that the abscess and hardness and pain and swelling and all other symptoms have subsided completely, and you need a plaster and bandaging, then repeat this as you did at the beginning in the same manner.
And if no cause occurs to the limb of those we have mentioned, then do not loosen it until after three days or four or ten or seven; and in some cases all this is left for twenty days according to what appears suitable for the bandaging of the limb, as we have said, until the fracture adheres, and the flesh coagulates over it.
In the hours adjust more tightly than in your first stricture altogether, and adjust according to the swelling of the patient's flesh, as was preceded by memory.
But if you see the site of the fracture already drying and withering more than is necessary, then you will know that nutrition cannot reach it; therefore, poultice that area with warm water every day until three days have passed; and loosen the bandage a little, and through this operation lead nourishment to the limb, and it will be healed quickly.
But what unskilled restorers of a fractured limb do otherwise when it is not set at the beginning as needed and is set according to its curvature, is an error in their procedure and great fear, and if this were correct, the Ancients would have enlarged on this in their books and practiced it accordingly.
And I have not seen any of them do that, which is why it is more reasonable to think that they should not do so.
Chapter Second: On fractures when they occur in the head.
The types of fractures are many, and their figures are diverse, and the causes are distinct. Some fractures are caused by impact with a hard object, where the impact removes all the bone within the circumference, which is behind the bone, just as it happens in wood; thus, this type is called a chisel fracture. Or the object may cut some part of the bone and also cut through the flesh without passing the incision to the back side; and this type of fracture is called absolute elevation.
And the severity of these two fractures can be great or slight, and there is also a fracture that is comminuted or crushed; the cause of this is impact with something weak or falling on a stone, and similar circumstances.
And this fracture is either penetrating that approaches the panniculus which is below the bone; or it is in the face of the bone, and the harm of these two fractures is great or little.
And of the fracture that is weakened in the bone, in the subtlety of the small hair-like parts, and for this reason this particular type of fracture is named capillary.
And it is a fracture that is made either by falling or by the impact of a stone and similar objects, which causes some plate of bone to enter into its parts, and it is made in the place of concavity just as it happens with the bones of the arm when they occur due to their impact and are broken upon entering into their parts.
And this is mostly made in the bones of children's bodies, just as it happens with the heads of infants.
And for all these kinds of fractures, treatments are used, which are remedies applied separately. And I will return to the reminder of all this in its place.
And you should recognize all these kinds of fractures by detection over them and by inquiry into their attempts and the appearance of corrupted flesh that is upon them.
And the capillary fracture is recognized by this: it is revealed on the bone, and when it is wiped clean, it appears as a black mark on the surface of the bone.
But in the treatment of fractures, consider at the beginning the symptoms of the patient, and if you see by these symptoms that there is significant danger, such as vomiting with anger, extension, loss of understanding, hoarseness of voice, fainting, acute fever, and similar severe conditions, and they appear in their most serious manifestations, then doubt whether healing will occur.
And if for unusual accidents you hope for safety, then you begin the treatment of them.
Which cause is that if it comes to you the plague at the beginning as it is plague and this is in the days of winter, then it is fitting that you study in the condition of the bone before the day xiiii according to all disposition.
And if it is in the days of summer, then it is fitting that you wait to remove the bone before the seventh day, since what is above the bone may become corrupted by inflammation, and that accidents which we have recounted may occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the membrane covering the brain and is in small pieces and inflamed, then it is fitting that you incise the injured and inflamed part, as I shall recount to you.
And it is that you shave the head of the patient who is afflicted, and uncover the bone according to custom and method as best you can according to the figure of the injury, so that it is clear on the patient.
And if it happens to you that you need the detection of the bone, bleeding or a cauterized abscess, then you must proceed with what is necessary, and this is that you cover the area with cloths infused in wine and rose oil, and allow the injury to rest until the abscess is calmed, and you are sure there will be no more bleeding; then begin the circumvention of the bone and its removal.
And this should be done according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you incise the bone with an incisor of narrow form, and this is the shape of it: And then administer another incisor, slightly wider than the first; and this is the shape of it:
And thus apply the poultice of the incision until the second day.
And it is necessary for you to know about various incisions, some of which are applied for longer periods than others; and some are applied more quickly than the others, and they vary in sharpness at their ends, and they are made of iron or flayed leather.
And use them with ease in tapping on the incisions so that you do not damage the head and cause harm to it.
And if the bone is hard and strong, it is necessary to perforate around it beforehand when you apply the incisions with the chisels, which are called non-deep chisels.
And they are not called thus only because these do not reach the thickness of the skull bone nearby, for the chisel has a rounded end or margin above it that is near the head like a border or small circle, which prevents it from being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone.
It is convenient for you to take many of these chisels, each of which suits the thickness of each bone, so that in all parts of the skull the tip of which is sharp, the quantity in length and brevity should be according to the thickness of this part.
And this is the form of three types of chisels: The quality of perforating around the broken bone is that you place the chisel on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated; then move the chisel to another location, and proceed in this manner throughout the entire length according to the thickness of the ray or about so.
Here is the translation:
And see that you keep all caution so that the incisor does not touch any part of the hip joint.
And thus when you have circled the bone to perforate it, and if it is continuous with the hip joint, you will extract it, so that you rasp and equalize its roughness to match that of the one in the bone using a tool similar to an incisor, but make sure it is finer and more delicate than other incisors.
And if any part of the small and delicate bones breaks, now take that with ease using what is appropriate among your tools; and then treat the wound with lint and herbs which we have mentioned.
And the other method of operation is light, distant from fear, which Guillia describes and which she praises highly; and this is what she says: It is necessary for you at the beginning to start uncovering the part of the bone in the place where the fracture is most severe and obvious, until when you have uncovered that part and paused with the tip of this slow and cheerful incisor whose form is as follows:
And it is that the lens-shaped part is left intact and no incision is made to it, while the sharp-edged part is divided into two sides along its length, as you see; so that the lens-shaped part fits against the hip joint with the sharp edge of the bone.
And then strike the incisor of one part with a small hammer until you have cut through all the bone with ease as it is revolved, and be sure to protect the panniculus so that no accident happens in any way that you fear might occur; and even if the physician were the most careless of all men and the least knowledgeable among them, or even asleep.
In such a case if any cause is attached to the bone of the abdomen in some places of bone, incise that from where the tip of the scalpel reaches, and deliver it from there where it is easier, for it is delivered without harm or fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the abdomen, and that which is abraded or remains of the bone is its surface, and it lies in roughness and small projections, then you should scrape off that roughness and eradicate those projections with fine razors which you have prepared in various quantities so that it may be possible for you to apply them where they are most appropriate and use them correctly according to how your operation on the broken bone and its figure proceeds.
And you should apply in your ratio with which you scrape the bone at first the roughest of those razors, and gradually use one finer than the other, and do not cease doing this continuously until you reach applying the thinnest and finest of all of them.
In each of the other small and fine fractures and subtle fracture, you should administer care according to what is needed for their correction.
And it is the cause that it is not feared by all those who have studied in that art. And regarding what we have written about has been shown in major fractures.
And if the abdomen is rectangular when you remove the bone, then take cloth according to the size of the wound, and soak it in warm oil, and place it over the wound opening; then take another folded or triple-layered cloth and soak it in wine and warm oil, and place it on top of the first cloth as lightly as possible so that you do not press on the surrounding tissue; then apply a thick bandage over it, but bind it only enough to cover the wound itself.
If you are certain of this cure within one or two days that there is no danger of an abscess forming; and then loosen that [dressing] and promptly administer medicines with which excessive dryness is harmful, such as lint pads and flour poultices, and powders of endive and arsene, and similar to those.
And it is that you should make these medicines into powder which is finely ground over the wound, just as they are dry; and generally, administer to it any medicine whose property is to cleanse without irritating.
And guard according to your virtue that the wound in your treatment be clean; and let it not become infected or worse; nor allow its virulence to increase. For when it is situated on the panniculus cerebri, it corrupts that area, and you will have to treat it promptly due to this malicious condition.
And if it affects the scalp, when the bone is cleansed and especially when pressure is applied to treat it, and there is blackness on its surface; and therefore consider at such times if you encounter the accidents we have mentioned, know that it is perilous with all caution.
And if it is not blackness but the medicine that was applied above which causes this discoloration, then take one part of honey and three parts of olive oil, and use these to moisten a piece of cloth, and then apply it to the scalp; and promptly treat it with the spices suitable for healing until it is healed.
Chapter III.
On the restoration of the nose when it is cut.
Know that the nose is not broken except in two main places, or one of those lesser places, because there you have two bones, since the lower part is cartilaginous, and it is not broken, but only inflamed, twisted, and swollen.
If one of the two main parts is broken, then you should insert your little finger into the side of the nose, and adjust that fracture from within with your index finger, and with your thumb outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And do this with ease, and be careful that your operation does not cause excessive pain to the patient.
And if the fracture is in the uppermost part of the nose, and your finger cannot reach it, then you should use the end of a probe with some thickness.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you should proceed as described.
And attempt to restore it on the first day of the fracture if possible; otherwise, wait until the seventh or tenth day when the swelling has subsided.
And insert into the end of the nose a pad of linen cloth if the fracture is in one place, or two pads if the fracture is in two places, and let the pad be thick enough to fill the cavity of the nose.
And some of the ancient restorers say that the pads should be soaked in butter, and changed daily, but I have not seen this done; rather, it is best to soak the pad in albumen of egg mixed with powdered sugar, and then leave it in place until the bone is set and the cartilage has hardened.
In some cases, insert in the nose, instead of a sponge, a piece of cotton wrapped in lion's skin; and let it be that the fracture of the nose is so severe that the patient is not prevented from breathing.
And this cause is not necessary for you; and if you wish, do that, and if you wish, use sponges.
And if another swelling occurs that needs to be cauterized, apply a plaster to the nose made of pitch or honey mixed with vinegar and rose oil, or use myrrh.
But if it does not require cauterization, now you should apply plasters to that area with sesame flour and powdered herbs mixed with egg white; and afterwards place them over the lion's skin, and do not cover the nose with anything.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or comminuted, now you should cut around them, and extract them with instruments that are suitable for this; and then close the gap, and treat it with substances that promote healing and consolidation, and with appropriate plasters.
And if there is an abscess inside the nose, now you should treat it with sponges and administer lead cannulas until it is completely healed.
Chapter IV.
On the restoration of the jaw when it is dislocated.
When the lower jaw is broken and there is no abscess at the fracture site, now observe if the fracture is only outward and not split into two parts and curved inward, then its diagnosis is straightforward.
Therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, you should insert your left finger into the mouth of the patient; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left side, then use your right finger; and push in such a way that you bring the bone fragments together from within with ease to the outside parts, using your other hand from the outside part of the bone; act appropriately in this manner.
And if the jaw fracture has already separated into two parts, then you should administer extension from both sides as much as possible to achieve proper alignment.
