And if there is a concussion of the teeth with disruption, then strengthen those that you expect will break with a thread of gold or silver or silk; and immediately apply wax over the fractured jaw, and then apply a double cloth pad, and place a properly made bandage or a piece of cloth equal in length to the jaw; and then bind it above as is appropriate for your binding material and for the application, so that it is not destroyed.
And command the patient to use rest and tranquility, and give him food in the form of thick soups.
And if you determine that the figure has already been altered in any way, then you should examine and release it on the third day; and then correct what has already been altered of it and apply a plaster with powdered linseed, egg white, or ground sesame, after you have removed the wax from it, and place a thick wool bandage over the plaster.
And then as you place and adjust the plaster on it, if the position of the bone is not altered, leave it without releasing it until it is healed and the fracture is strengthened; for this fracture typically strengthens in about three weeks.
And if another abscess should occur after cautery, then administer what we have mentioned for other cases in the treatment of the interior until it is healed, and let that abscess be brought to a head.
And if the fracture is a splinter, now consider whether it is a piece of bone separated or shattered, now you must use subtlety to extract those fragments with what is convenient for their removal.
And if the opening of the wound is narrow, now enlarge it with a scalpel according to the quantity needed.
And when you have extracted those fragments, and if they do not come to a head by themselves, now close the opening of the wound if it is wide; and if not, apply one of the plasters that are suitable for this purpose until it becomes inflamed, until it is healed.
Chapter 5.
On the restoration of the collarbone when it is broken.
There are various ways the collarbone can be broken at the front part beyond the muscle, and this fracture occurs in one of three manners.
For it may be broken, or separated, or separated into two parts without fragments; and this is easy for restoration. Or it happens that fragments occur, making its restoration difficult. Or it is such that the fracture involves a splinter.
And the procedure in such a case, when the fracture involves a splinter, is that you have two assistants present; one to hold the splintered collarbone and guide it, and the other to extend the neck towards that part.
For adjusting the fracture with the said instruments, ensure that the shape is made according to what is needed, and not in a slight prominence or depression.
And if you need more extension, now it is necessary to place beneath the tilt of the malaligned bone a pad made of cloth or wool, and its size should be according to the quantity needed; extend and elevate the clavicle and compress the pad with your hand so that you adjust the fracture as required.
And if you cannot pull the clavicle's extremity to the outer parts, so that it reaches its proper position, now it is necessary for the patient to be reclined on their neck, and place a pad over the muscle on one side, about half in size.
And the assistant presses the muscle on the lower parts so that the bone of the clavicle is elevated to its proper position, and now you should correct the fracture and adjust it with the said instruments.
But if you feel protrusions of the clavicle and roughness, now it is necessary to cut into those areas and remove those protrusions with ease.
And if it is held in the bone, now you must devise an incision with one of the scalpel blades that you have prepared for this purpose, after placing beneath the clavicle the tool used for drilling; and this tool is made of wood or metal, having the following form:
And it resembles the bottom of a bowl, with this being its concavity; and its width should be according to what you need from it, depending on the size of the bone or its smallness.
Make it as long as seems suitable to you for the operation; let it have two ends, just as times require, and one end be wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound which you have incised when you extracted the splinter from the bone is wide, and you are sure it will form an abscess, then you now adjust the two lips of the wound with a suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear it will form an abscess too soon, then now employ the wound with patches and powders according to the quantity of the wound's cleft.
And if an abscess forms soon, then now apply a patch in fresh oil and rose water, and place it over that.
And do not let the splint be loose above the broken bone; and therefore it is necessary that you see the patient every day, so that when the bandaging is soft or you see the splint already loosened, then you must correct this and tighten the binding.
And place the patient's pillow at the back of his neck, and place underneath it a small cushion to raise his head during sleep; therefore lift the support under his armpits, so that the shoulder blade fracture is raised according to the muscle elevation; or bind his arm to his neck.
And do not remove the bandage unless it is necessary due to an abscess or swelling, within twelve days.
And then change the plaster if you have seen the face of it, and adjust it in the joint and restore it, and leave it until it is set and the shoulder blade fracture is healed.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the method in twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some people it is done in less time.
Chapter VI.
On the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
The scapula is broken in the middle part and not at its edges.
And when it is broken in that place — and it is broken in the middle part — and it is known only by palpation, then according to the shape of the fracture prepare yourself and reduce it according to its natural figure in every way that is possible for you.
And then apply powdered alum of hauv and lion's fat; and then apply powder made from doubled cloth; and then apply over the plate a thin layer of ladanum according to the quantity of the spoon, all or at least a little.
And if the plate is concave in some places of the spoon, then you fill that concavity with lion's fat, and then spread the plate according to equality.
And then apply strips of linen soaked in hot liquid with proper constriction and with firmness to the plate so that it is not displaced from its place.
And consider the bandaging each day; and every time it is softened, change it another day and tighten the plate if it has been displaced from its place.
And place the dressing on its side as appropriate.
And the spoon is restored in xx days or in .xxv.
When this number is complete, then you loosen the bandage, and ensure that you know which of these are bones for which the beginning is not feared nor their dissolution.
And if you proceed to the place of the bone piece and place the bandage below it, then you cut above it, remove it, and do as we have said regarding the fracture of the furcula or suppuration or cauterization should that occur.
Chapter VII.
On the restoration of a fractured foot.
The foot sometimes is broken in the middle and remains there; but the ends move apart like the legs of a cicada, becoming separated and comminuted.
And the effects when the middle of it is broken are that it inclines towards the internal parts and causes great pain, difficulty in movement, coughing, fever, and vomiting blood; and a cavity is formed in the broken bone and this is not hidden from sensation.
And the restoration of it is that the patient lies supine on their back, and a pillow is placed between the shoulders; then gently press the muscles, and align the ribs with more than two rib pieces, and carefully perform the operation and adjustment in every possible way that suits this case until the figure of the bone returns as needed.
And then place on it a plaster and cotton, and lay thin strips of wood or reeds or similar light materials over them, wrapping all in cloth; then gently bind that around the broken bone without pressing too hard; and apply bandages to the back several times, tightening them appropriately; and afterward, consider the bandage at all times, and when it has loosened, tighten it again.
And if necessity compels the removal of that which has been corroded or protruded due to pain, swelling, or abscess that occurred in the area, then you should cut and loosen it and remove the plaster and correct what happened according to this method of treatment; and afterwards apply the plaster again, if it is appropriate for the condition; and adjust the bandaging until it is healed.
Chapter VIII.
On the restoration of ribs when they are broken.
Know that a fracture of the ribs does not occur except in thick areas where they join; and at their extremities in front, such contusions do not happen to them because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of it is not hidden from the feeling, but the examination of it among those mentioned.
And the restoration should be such that it equalizes the fracture among those mentioned according to the possible method, until it is set as needed, and then apply plaster and bind the broken bone with a splint if necessary.
And if the fracture of the ribs inclines toward the inner parts, then severe pain and piercing occur, similar to the pain that happens when one weeps, because the bone pierces the membrane; and there is difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting blood; and this is difficult to treat.
And the Ancients were skilled in that with powerful remedies.
And what they say is: it is necessary to avoid foods that cause inflammation and gas, so that the abdomen becomes swollen and extended, and thus the fracture is pushed outward.
Let us proceed with this by which there will not be reason to cause an inflamed abscess through cauterization, if it does not occur; and if it has already occurred, now help is given for that.
And some of them say it should be placed above the windpipe and near where it draws in strongly; but this is similar to reasoning, yet it is feared that the windpipe might carry the superfluities to the place due to its weakness.
And some of them say: cover the place with wool that has already been submerged in hot oil, and place poultices between the ribs until it is filled, so that the bandaging follows the shape as when wrapping it according to its roundness; and nearby treat the ailment with the cure for pleurisy using food and medicine.
And if the cause of the ailment is violent constraint that cannot be endured, and the bone pierces through the membrane at a point, we fear for the ailment; now it should be that we cut above the location, expose the broken rib, and then place over it an instrument to hold the incision, the use of which was previously mentioned, and that they saw the bone with ease, and extract it.
And then we adjust the two edges of the wound, if it is large, by stitching, and treat it with plasters until it heals.
And if an abscess occurs internally, now you should apply heat and press poultices in rose oil onto the area, and treat the ailment with what is appropriate for an internal abscess.
And he should lie on his side on the bed until he is healed.
Chapter IX.
On the restoration of fractures of the thoracic and cervical spine.
In some cases, fractures occur in the cervical vertebrae, and they heal slowly, similarly to the thoracic spine. And when this happens to someone and you want to know if they will recover or not, at that time consider whether their muscles are already softened, numb, and dead; if they cannot move or stretch them, and when you press on those areas with your fingers or pierce them with a needle and no pain is felt there, then you know that they will not recover, and in such cases it is fatal.
And if you move those areas and feel constriction with the fingers and pain upon piercing them, then you know that the vertebra is safe and that the injury will heal with treatment.
And if the cervical vertebrae show similar symptoms and you want to know if they will recover or not, at that time consider the condition of their legs.
If you see that the legs are already softened and observe similar symptoms in them as those affecting the upper body, and shortly after lying on their back they experience vomiting and involuntary evacuation, and sometimes when lying on their back they want to urinate but cannot, then you know it is fatal, and at that point there is no need for treatment.
But if none of those symptoms occur, the cause is better and more remote.
And the treatment for them is to apply a poultice to reduce the swelling over the fractured vertebra using raw oil or boiled honey mixed with flour; place this on the affected area until the swelling subsides.
During the hours of rest, apply one of the comforting and stimulating plasters, and bind it over that part; and command the patient to use repose and tranquility and not to sleep on the side where pain is felt until it is healed.
And if it happens that the marrow of the bone is exposed or any cause has separated it from its place, then cover it with a plaster and place that bone; then bring together the two lips of the wound, using sutures if it is large; and thereafter treat it with one of the incarnative ointments until it is healed.
But if the end of the bone is broken and it is the tip of the limb, then cover it with the fingers of your left hand in the buttocks, and align the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible by second-degree reduction; and thereafter apply a plaster and splint, if you need them, and bind it.
And if you feel exposed fractures in it, then act on that, remove them, and treat the wound with the treatment that has been prescribed until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
On the restoration of the broad [structure].
The bones of the broad structures are often broken, and when they break, the fracture in them is not a complete break at their extremities but rather a split along their length and a displacement to the sides; and as a result, the patient experiences pain in the location and puncture sensation, and movement is restricted when it is fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over it until the fracture assumes the figure of its natural position.
And if the fracture is at the extremity of the limb, then adjust it as suits you by reduction so that it resembles its natural shape.
And if the fracture is in the long bone that inclines toward the lower parts, then make the patient lie on their belly so that you can prepare the restoration of that fracture. And when you have adjusted it, apply a plaster over it, and then place a splint of wood or cane beside it, and bind it firmly with bandages in such a way that you do not fear any puncture of the fracture or movement of the splint.
And adjust the concavity by filling it so that it conforms to equality, and instruct the patient to sleep on their back or on their side.
And if an acute abscess occurs, then stop the extension and restoration of it until the abscess is brought to a head, and apply what is appropriate for that condition as it has developed; and then return to the restoration of it, and bind it as is necessary.
And if the bones are displaced or crushed, then you must treat them.
Chapter XI.
On the restoration of the fracture of the adjunct bone.
The adjunct is what is located between the spine and the top of the shoulder.
And if it is fractured, then the restoration of it is in one of two ways, one of which is that you take a flat piece of wood of moderate thickness in this form:
Let there be two ends of bandages, and let it be suspended from a high place.
Lay the patient on a chair; then place the broken arm outside onto the stick until it reaches the midpoint where the curvature of the stick is attached.
Then suspend something heavy from it, or have the patient extend it downward with their hands; and then the physician should place the fracture along its natural lines until the fracture returns to its proper position.
Another method is to have the patient lie on their back with their neck raised and suspend the hand of the neck with bandages; then instruct two assistants that one should hold what is above the fracture by its ends, and the other should hold below him; and each should pull in their respective directions.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then wrap bandages over the fracture on one side and over it again on the other side, and have each of the two assistants pull in their respective directions.
And if the fracture is close to the end of the muscle, then it is necessary to place half of the bandage over the knot and the other part below the fracture towards the joint.
And similarly, if the fracture is close to the joint, then it is necessary to place the bandage over that location and around that joint.
Tie it in this manner with ease and effort, and then when it is tied properly as needed and applied well, secure it tightly unless an abscess occurs.
If pus forms in an abscess and is drained, within seven days there should be no stricture; then pause on placing linen strips soaked in vinegar and rose oil until the abscess rests, and thereafter strengthen it.
And the method to strengthen it is to apply a plaster over the fracture, and then wrap it with wet cloth over the plaster; then adjust the arm on that support, and place its hand over its own muscle.
And place the cloth and strengthen over the support for the arm so that the arm is in a fixed position, unless some cause of the broken bone prohibits this.
And now use splints.
And it is that you place on the fracture that splint which is the widest and strongest of the other splints; and place between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the break plus three fingers in total from all sides.
And then strengthen over the splints with the support which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your support over the site of the fracture should be tighter. And the farther the fracture is from the joint, the smaller the support should be.
And thus, when you position the splints and the support as we have said at the time when you restore the limb to its proper position, then do so.
And if you fear that pus will form in the abscess, then leave the support and the splints for seven days as we have said.
Then consider the bandage in all parts for three days so that inflammation or pus does not occur properly or by swelling; or it is forbidden for food to reach the member due to excess or constriction; and thus correct everything as we have said.
If you are confident that no cause of this will occur, then do not loosen the bandage for at least three days or more.
And let the patient sleep on their back, and place their hands on their stomach.
And place a pad filled with wool on the helper, and at all times of the night or day, ensure that the shape of the broken limb is not loosened or dissolved, or that the bandage is too loose; thus, correct this with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order we mentioned before, such that the food is light at first until healing begins; then it is appropriate to make the diet richer.
For the custom of the splint and cast is to be tight for forty days; then it should be loosened, a bath administered, and treated with ointments that correct this.
And if the fracture is healing by rubbing together, then do not loosen the bandage and the splints for one day or even two months.
Chapter XII.
On the restoration of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called the ulna and radius; one of which is small and is that which lies under the little finger, and the other is larger and is placed below the smaller one, towards the lower part.
If only the larger bone or the smaller one is broken, and if they are disjointed.
And when the smaller bone is broken above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is quicker.
And when the bone below is broken, the fracture is severe, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when two bones are broken at once.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the smaller one above, then it is appropriate for the physician, due to its easy restoration, to allow a small extension with ease until it is aligned.
But if the larger bone is the one that is broken, then it is necessary to allow a more vigorous extension.
And if both bones are broken at once, then it is necessary to allow the most vigorous extension.
And it is necessary to place the hand in position for its restoration and extension stretched out on a board, with the wrist above all the fingers, except that the thumb should be lower than the other fingers, and the patient should lie on a cushioned bed.
And there should be a board in front of it at the height or directly in line with it, so that the patient does not have to strain.
And the attendant should extend the arm from below with his hand or bandage, and another attendant should extend it similarly from above.
And the physician should align the bone so that it is set in the best position possible.
And if in the setting of the bone you stagger, then at that hour reduce all parts to their place with your skill.
And if it appears in the injury separately that the bone is displaced and it is not to your hope for its restoration, then at that hour you act upon it and remove it according to the method which we have said above.
But if the fracture is with infection, I have already made a separate chapter for that, and so take the treatment of it.
And if an abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration, then you apply a cloth with cerot made of rose oil and white wax, and it should be between thickness and fineness, and place a bandage over it with gentle pressure so that the abscess subsides; and then remove the cerot and apply a plaster prepared from ground powder mixed with egg whites, and afterward place poultices; and let the poultice placed over that fracture be wider and firmer.
And know that the number of poultices for the arm varies in major dispositions, or it is a fracture in one joint or in two joints together.
And then place your bandage over the location of the fracture more firmly and vigorously, and when it is necessary to loosen the bandaging on the parts above the fracture or on the parts below it, place the bandage a bit looser according to how it was described at the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths that you wrap around the fracture should be soft and moistened cloths, not hard or rough, and the bandage with which it is wrapped should be of a proper medium between fine and thick, as we have recommended.
And consider the limb and the bandaging for some days. And if any cause arises for which correction is necessary, such as when inflammation occurs in the limb, you should immerse the limb in hot water until that inflammation subsides.
And do not force the limb until it rests, and then return to the bandaging.
And if the dislocation has already been reduced and the bone is already in place and appears to be so, then correct all of this with your skill.
And consider whether the nutrition is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive bandaging, for then you should loosen that slightly and leave it for some days until nutrition reaches it, and then strengthen that.
But if the patient does not experience any of the causes we have mentioned, then it is not necessary to remove the bandage for about twenty days or thereabouts.
And then have the patient's arm suspended by their own skill of agitated movements, and make them lie down to sleep on their back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days or thirty-two days, or sometimes it may be restored in twenty-eight days, and all of this depends on the condition of the tissues and the strength of the patient.
Chapter XIII.
On the restoration of a fracture of the thumb and of the fingers.
Know that at the base of the thumb and at the joints of the fingers, when there is a fracture, it rarely leads to severe complications.
When it happens that the bone is fractured or dislocated, at once it is necessary that the patient lie still and face up on the bed according to equality, and place the arm on top of that extended.
And then the minister should extend the broken bones and the surgeon should adjust them until they are properly set.
And now it is necessary to apply a plaster and cotton, unless an abscess occurs which requires cauterization; and then place a piece of wood above according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped that in linen.
But if the fracture is in the parts inside the palm of the hand, and now make it appear as a sphere of cloth, and command the patient to hold that with their fractured palm; then bind it with cloth.
And let the piece of wood be curved with cloth in which it is elevated, so that the curve is applied to the palm; and hold that as necessary.
But if the fracture is in the parts outside, now it is necessary to place a piece of wood above and another piece below on the palm, so that the hand is supported and straight; and then bind it, as you would wrap the fingers with bandages.
And if there is a fracture in one of the bones of the fingers, now if it is the thumb, adjust it as necessary and then bind it to the palm; and if you wish, place a small straight splint so that the fracture is elevated and not injured.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as in the middle finger, or in the ring finger, or in the little finger, now it should be adjusted and wrapped to the adjacent finger; or all of them should be wrapped together continuously, which is better; or place a small splint over it, as we have said for the thumb.
And consider, in your restoration, and approach that, the disposition of an abscess cut and opposed — both the cold operation — both with what is needed when some cause of those occurs, as it is repeated the narration of them.
Chapter XIV.
On the restoration of a fracture of the bone in the forearm.
The bone of the forearm is mostly broken and is evident to the touch according to how it has shifted forward and upward.
And the restoration of it is done in such a way that one bandage is stretched over the fracture and another bandage below the fracture, and the patient lies prone on their stomach and face.
And the attendant should extend all the bandages to their proper place according to the quality when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if it is closer to the elbow of the forearm, then the bandage should be placed toward the back at the part of the elbow, so that the extension stops there, and the other bandage below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture is closer to the back, then the bandage should be placed toward the back, and the extension should be made at the parts below.
And then the physician should equalize the bone on both sides as much as possible so that it is reduced according to its natural appearance and the bone is joined by good union; now it is necessary to apply plaster and bandaging, if an abscess does not occur in the limb. But if an abscess does occur, then you should leave it for several days until the cold abscess subsides, and then return to your treatment.
The binding of the splint should be done as follows: wrap around the fracture a hard bandage, and fold it two or three times, and trim off the excess; then wrap the limb up to the ankle and the root of the thigh.
And insert a long strip of cloth between the flesh and the splint near the end towards the lower parts, and let the ends of the cloth extend from the upper part of both sections, and then wrap around the splint and the limb whatever excess remains of the bandage.
And then place over the limb at the site of the fracture splints, and lay one strip over the bone of the splint.
And then fill the spaces between the splint and the limb with soft cloths, so that the binding is well padded.
And then begin a strong binding directly over the fracture with three or four turns, and as you move away from the site of the fracture using the bandage, your binding should become looser, lighter, and softer.
And then extend the two ends of the cloth towards the lower parts until it reaches the ankle, and tie both ends of the strips from the other side so that the bandage does not become loose from its place.
And then leave the binding in place as it is, so that no corrosion, abscess, swelling, or similar conditions occur on the limb; and if any such cause should arise, cut through the bandage at that point and correct what has occurred as soon as possible, as we have said before.
If there is in the bone pus of the bone which must be evacuated, then you should open it as soon as possible; and if not, place a poultice on it and incise that, and treat with whatever preceded the recall until it is healed.
And in some cases, this fracture of the skull is strengthened by other things that are attached to it, as we have said about auxiliary plates and in the arm.
However, in the restoration of the limb in such a manner made, a bad limp does not occur; and if it is restored alone, or something else applied to the other side, then it is necessary that the person limps forever.
And know that the skull is strengthened in forty days or a few more days, and all this depends on the diversity of complexions and other conditions.
Chapter XV.
On the restoration of the fracture of the kneecap.
Know that the fracture of the kneecap often occurs in this manner, but it also happens due to various other causes.
However, if it is a fracture, then it is merely a crack or bruise in its parts, and this is either one injury or several injuries, and everything about this has been stated concerning the sensation.
And the restoration of it is such that what is broken in its parts and among the aforementioned should be opened so that it may be joined and conjoined as much as possible by equalization, facilitation, and fabrication.
Apply the plaster and place it on that round bandage if you need one; then stretch it above tightly to fit appropriately.
And consider all the positions that we have recommended for other fractures, as well as abscesses and similar conditions. Apply these instructions according to what is appropriate until the injury is healed.
Chapter XVI.
On the restoration of a hip fracture.
Know that in the hip there are two bones, one of which is thick and is called the hip bone; the other is slender and is called the femur.
And when any of the types of fracture occur to both bones as happens with the two bones of the arm, then the restoration is done in the same way as for the arm, and the operation is the same.
And if you join the two bones together, the hip is stabilized in all directions.
And if the more slender bone is fractured, the hip is stabilized in the forward directions.
And if the thicker bone is fractured, you will find it below, and these are causes that are not within your control.
And then administer extension, alignment, and binding of the fragments accordingly.
However, be careful if the hip fracture is jagged with multiple fragments; then the extension should be gentle and done with great caution.
And when there is any cause for surgery on the arm, and you have aligned the bones and completed all of the necessary operation, then you will take pieces of wood from trees that are peeled below their bases, and those which are made similarly to palm branches; choose among these those with slight grooves, and not those with ridges like fingers, and make their length according to the length of the arm, and insert them deep into the pus beneath.
And then wrap around each one a cloth in two layers according to its length, and place one at the end of the arm on one side and the other on the opposite side, and they should be inserted deep into the pus at the proper location.
And then bind the two bones in three places at both ends and in the middle, for with this structure the arm is prevented from tilting to either the right or left side, and thus remains in proper position.
And now a wooden brace has been applied according to the length of the arm; and it should be placed such that the arm can still move appropriately.
And especially do this in the case of a fracture, and when it is properly a case of bruising as well.
And then consider each day the arm and study it carefully based on previous observations, and you should guard against abscesses and inflammation or other similar causes; and when any of these causes occur, then act upon them appropriately until it is healed.
Chapter XVII.
Of the fracture of the heel and of the bones of the foot and of the aforementioned.
To the scalp does not happen a fracture entirely; but to the bones of the foot happens some fractures; and to the said bones of the foot few happens a fracture, and does not happen to them except healing in the manner of disposition.
And if to the bones of the foot happens a fracture and times those bones be raised up above themselves between them, then the patient should rest his foot on the ground prepared thusly as mentioned.
And place one foot on what is raised by those bones, and then press on them, and compress them, and bind them until they return to their places.
And then apply a plaster and place it above, and below the arch of the foot place a small board with two padded ends.
And then strengthen that with the foot in a proper position, since you have wrapped it with cloth and other materials as needed.
And as three or four days pass, then you loosen the bandage, for you will find the bones compressed, or broken or dislocated, and the treatment for these conditions is as you know.
But if any of them are broken, then restore those, and compress them as I have recommended to you in the restoration of the bones of the hand. And then place the broken bone in a splint according to the length of the finger, and make it somewhat wider; and below the foot place the board as recommended to you, and strengthen that with proper positioning.
But if what is broken are two or three or more of the bones, then you place on each finger a splint of its appropriate length wrapped in soft cloth, and secure the board at the bottom of the foot so that it rests on one end of the board under the arch of the foot for proper support.
It is fitting for you not to forget that you should not disregard all the causes which are mentioned to you in the other fractures of the accidents we have spoken about; and pay attention to each incident concerning this as well.
Chapter XVIII.
On the fracture of the bone of the female pelvis and of the bone of the penis and of the shaft of the male.
When the bone of the female pelvis is fractured, at that moment perform the caesarean section while she lies flat, and then slightly incline her to the side on one side, and have someone hold her from behind.
And then the midwife fills the pelvis with cotton, and it should be done in the pelvis as if for a regular delivery; and then the woman should be urged to push, elevating the buttocks little by little, because all of this is intended so that she faces the opening of the pelvis as if for a normal delivery and the fractured bone returns.
And then place poultices on her buttocks, and when she wants to urinate, remove the cotton to facilitate it, and after urination reduce and return that according to the method by which you managed it at the beginning, and then return to her original poultice.
And do this for seven days or around that time, because it takes that long to restore.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and tie one end of a reed to it; then insert the entire bladder into her pelvis, and then blow air through the reed until the bladder is inflated inside the pelvis, because this helps the fracture to return.
Let it be filled with cotton and dressed for several days as we have recommended, until it is healed.
And when the bone of the thumb of a man or woman is broken, immediately administer for its restoration and alignment what we have recommended for the thigh bone.
And what is right is not concealed in these nearby fractures that rarely occur, especially for one who has some study and use of this my book; for such a fracture signifies to you in the major dispositions via directness in its restoration and alignment, and thus you understand well.
But in the arm of a man when it is broken, take a sling made of leather, and put the arm in that; then place it on padding of cloth, and wrap it up, and afterwards leave it for three days or about that, until it is healed.
Chapter XIX.
On the restoration of bone fractures when there is swelling.
It is convenient for one who suffers a fracture with swelling, and especially if the bone is large such as the femur or the humerus, or similar to these, that you apply cupping and incise it immediately if the conditions for incising indicate this to you as we have said above.
And if the swelling emits blood, then it is necessary that you apply cupping to retain it in such a way that you powder on it powdered eggshell, unless another cause is present.
And then begin to restore the fracture on that same day, and do not delay this, unless an abscess occurs.
If an abscess is cauterized, then for seven days you must refrain from the restoration of its interior until it subsides, and do not approach it on the third day and the fourth, because you will cause harmful accidents.
If the broken bone protrudes above the skin, then you should attempt to reduce it and align it with ease and little extension.
If extension is not possible with your hands and the alignment must be done with tools, then you reduce it using this instrument; and this instrument is made of iron, its length being seven or eight digits and its width according to the size of the wound; and therefore, it is necessary for the surgeon to take three or four of these, depending on what is needed for various types of fractures.
And let it be round, and let it have little groove so that it does not bend due to the pressure upon it during the operation.
And let it have sharp ends with margins at its extremities; and let the top of it be thick, and from the middle inward to the parts.
And this is the form of it:
And it is called in Greek "labrum voluntas atelarem," which means a small palette.
It is necessary for the extremity to be placed at rest with it elevated above the protruding end of the bone.
And press it in one motion until the bone returns to its place and is aligned with some degree of alignment.
Therefore, try to align the ends of the broken bone between them, and if the end of the fractured part is slender, and you do not find that instrument suitable for it, then you should insert the end of that bone into where the instrument has power over it.
If you cannot reduce the bone entirely as we have recommended, then insert that part with one of the saws which we have mentioned; or cut that part with one of the saws in whatever way is possible for you.
And smooth that which remains rough on the bone and small jagged parts.
And when the bone is reduced, and after the reduction the patient feels intense pain in the tooth, then you know that the bone has not returned to its natural place; and then do what will be of great help to the patient.
When your restoration of the bone is complete, then soak a cloth in black styptic wine, and if it is summer, do not apply cerotic or any other substance that contains oil on the wound, so that it does not cause putrefaction and corruption.
And then administer poultices during the appropriate time of your bone restoration, and leave the wound uncovered so that you can insert a probe with forceps and use bandages according to the size of the wound.
And be careful not to touch the wound near the fracture, for many times unskilled doctors have done this, and it has caused death or serious illness to their patients, which is harmful. And if the swelling is soft, flexible, different from the swelling of other fractures.
And if the swelling is large or is a bad swelling, and you fear some of the maladies that we have recounted, and you feel pain in the area, then it is not necessary to wait on those splints, but make an envelope of hard cloths instead of splints and bind them there.
And when after one day or two the swelling begins to subside and it is evident that pus has formed, then you remove the cloth which you had placed over the bandage, and promptly apply poultices, such as those we customarily use for treating swellings, such as tetrafarmacum and similar remedies.
And it is necessary for you to loosen the bandage and examine the swelling every morning and evening until it is consolidated and firm, and it is important that the limb be prepared in such a way that the pus can drain from it downward with ease.
If several days have passed and the swelling has not consolidated nor has pus drained from it, then you should know that here there is a delay due to small particles that need to be removed from the swelling. And of those delays, those that are separated should be removed and extracted. For those from which they cannot be separated and cause pain in the limb, perforate them as much as possible according to what is feasible for you.
And if the swelling becomes inflamed or infected, which are signs of inflammation, or another form of corruption and putrefaction, then it is necessary to apply one of those remedies mentioned in the preceding chapter on the treatment of such conditions.
And when the bone must be cut, it protrudes above the limb like the bones of the chest and shoulder and similar to those of larger limbs, and do not attempt traction on it, nor its extraction for fear that this might lead to death after four movements; wait until it softens inside, as it will easily fall out in about twenty or thirty days; and during this time care for the wound if it is visible, and otherwise leave it alone.
Chapter XX.
On the treatment of knotting that occurs in the aftermath of some fracture.
This knotting often occurs in the aftermath of the healing of a fracture, especially near the joints, and disfigures the shape of the limb; and it significantly impedes the normal function of the limb.
Then consider whether the knotting is recent and administer medicines that have a styptic effect such as aloes, sesame, myrrh, saffron, and similar ones; take any of these or all of them, and mix them with vinegar to make them styptic, or with egg white, or with vinegar to make them astringent, and apply this over the knotting on cloth, and bind it tightly over it with proper compression.
And keep the compression on without loosening it for several days; then loosen it and reapply it until the knotting is softened; or apply a sheet of lead gently over it, as lead has the property of reducing swelling in limbs.
But if the knotting has already turned to bone and is causing tightness and necessity demands that it be removed quickly, then cut it at its weakest point, and excise the excess or reduce it with some remedies until it softens; and treat it until it heals.
Chapter XXI.
On the restoration of fractures when they are set and the limb is restrained without conforming to its natural condition.
When a fracture is restored and the limb is restrained, it is done for various reasons, among which one reason is excessive manipulation of the bandaging and binding not according to what is necessary; or by excess in constriction of the bandages preventing nourishment from reaching the member; or by excessive application of poultices; or by unnecessary movement at an inappropriate time; or due to a lack of blood in the patient's body and weakness.
And the treatment for this is to give food to the patient and make their body supple so that blood is multiplied in them; administer baths; and apply poultices and leeches to them.
And apply plasters to the limb, so that the plaster attracts nourishment, or continue applying poultices with warm water, until nourishment flows and returns it to its natural form.
Chapter XXII.
On the treatment of broken bones when they are restored incorrectly and prevent proper healing from taking place.
When it happens that a limb, already restored near healing, shows deformity of the broken bone with swelling and nodosity, and this is due to a change in the form of the limb, but the limb is not prevented from its natural function, then you should not heed the words of those who believe that the limb is newly fractured.
Let this be known to all doctors and restorers who have performed this in our land; and this operation is highly reprehensible, leading to great fear, the sign of which is fatal.
If lameness and limping are recent, it is appropriate to bathe them in warm water containing certain soft herbs, such as althea roots, linden flowers, and melilot, or similar; and apply a soothing plaster or poultice properly prepared; or take mucilage made from althea root mixed with boiled linseed oil or olive oil, and apply it; or crush fatty figs in dove’s milk or something similar of the medicines known as consolidating remedies.
And in some cases, it is dissolved through frequent gentle friction, which should be applied to the most humid areas, and movement of the limb should be administered to all parts at all times.
But if the tortuosity has already advanced and become hard and turned into bone, and there is a need for treatment with iron, then it must be ensured that the pus above is drained, and the continuation of the bone is dissolved, and what is superfluous from the nodules of that part with the bone should be cut using fine instruments, and ease in this should be provided through diligence and study.
Chapter XXIII.
On Dislocation of Joints.
Dislocation is the wrongful displacement of any of the joints from its proper place, which prohibits movement, distorts the figure of the limb, and causes severe pain and great injury.
When a dislocation occurs, it must be immediately reduced without delay, for if reduction is delayed, the joint becomes inflamed and swollen, making the reduction of the dislocation difficult.
And for this reason it is not good to move or keep the patient standing during the inflammatory stage, as this often causes spasms and pain in the teeth.
But when this happens, you must prepare for the incision of the patient, and then allow them to rest until the inflammation subsides; afterwards, apply warm water and oil to the limb, and then reduce it with ease.
And treat the entire limb with what will help its recovery and return to normal.
And now I will order the chapters on dislocation as they were preceded by fractures; starting from the upper part of the body and moving to the lower part.
Chapter XXIIII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
When the two jaws are dislocated, they do not delay in treatment; and the dislocation of both can occur in one of two ways: either they are displaced from their positions by slight movement and slightly relaxed; or they are completely dislocated and fully out of place, to the point where they are relaxed at the joint where saliva flows from the patient's mouth and where they cannot close it, and the tongue is wrapped around its position.
But when the dislocation is slight, it often returns to its normal position on its own with little cause.
If the dislocation is complete and perfect, it must be reduced immediately, and not delayed at all.
And it is that the minister hold the patient's head, and insert the doctor’s two fingers into the cavity of the dislocated jaw from inside the mouth if the dislocation is on one side; or insert both thumbs if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers remain outside in a similar manner; and instructs the patient to cease moving their jaws and relax them entirely; and the doctor equalizes the jaw and pushes it back until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated, then administer inhalation with hot water and oil until the reduction can be performed gently on both sides, and do not delay the reduction of both sides at all, as we have said.
And when we return them and they are aligned, and the patient's mouth is closed and not relaxed, then place small pieces of cloth covered in wax on them, and make it from beeswax and oil softened; and afterward bind it with bandages gently.
And let the patient sleep on their back, and their head be positioned between both ears, so that it does not turn to the right or left; and do not force them to chew for any reason; have their food cut up finely until such time as when the pains are relieved and the jaws are healed, then allow them to do what they wish.
And this should be administered with bandages, and do not work on the opening of the mouth for eating, drinking, or speaking until the jaw is healed and cured.
And if the reduction of two dislocated jaws is difficult at any time and they are not returned to their places, fevers often occur frequently, and the patient's belly is easily distended, and he vomits pure bile.
And when you see this, then you know it is lost; and most often that person dies from this in ten days.
Chapter XXV.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the clavicle and the extremity of the muscle.
The clavicle is not dislocated from the chest inside towards the hands; and sometimes it is dislocated outwardly, and this is apparent by feeling.
And the restoration of it is that the patient lies on his back and extends his arms. And then press the area with your palm firmly, for it will return.
And then apply a plaster and powder to that area.
But the extremity of that which joins the collarbone is not dislocated on any day; therefore, it should be reduced and bandaged as we have said and as you are prepared.
And then apply a plaster, powder, and bandaging to that area, and instruct the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until he is healed.
And with this method of treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter XXVI.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated except in three ways, one of which is when it is dislocated from the upper part to the lower part.
The second is when it is dislocated above the muscle, and this happens quickly; and it is not dislocated behind by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated in front by the nerves.
It is most often dislocated outward below the tendon, and more particularly in those whose flesh is thin, it dislocates easily; but in those with thick flesh, it remains outside with difficulty.
It often happens to some people due to a blow or a fall that the muscle becomes swollen and inflamed, and as a result, it is likely to become dislocated.
Then you should understand this before you start treating it, and proceed with its treatment.
And the dislocation is known when it is below the tendon; therefore, you should distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the normal one, as there is a clear difference between them; you will find a depression at the head of the muscle below the tendon upon touch, and the patient cannot move their hand to their ear, nor can they make any such movement.
If it is dislocated below the foot or above, you will clearly feel it upon touch, and it is not concealed.
And from this dislocation an easy reduction is made when it is fresh and the patient is a child.
And the reduction is that the surgeon lifts the hand of the patient to the upper parts; then he places his fingers below the tip of the finger and elevates the joint forcefully back to its proper place, and the surgeon raises the hand of the patient, and extends it as needed; then he holds it down until it returns easily.
But if it does not return as we have said, and from the time of the initial dislocation for several days, baths in warm water should be given and an ointment should be administered to soften and line as if for rheumatism, using dittany, fenugreek, and melilot in water, and it should be applied.
And then the patient should be placed on their back and held below the tip of the finger in anticipation of it becoming equally between laxity and tension, and then the physician places his hand under the ball, elevates the muscle upwards, and pulls the patient's hand so that it is not injured below, while another assistant holds the head of the patient to prevent injury below, for it will quickly return to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it in this manner: have present a man who is taller than the patient, and let him stand on that side, and insert his arm below the tip of the patient's finger and elevate the tip upwards while the patient hangs in the air, and another assistant pulls the patient’s hand from below their abdomen. But if the patient is light, then it is necessary to have something else suspended for them to hang from, so that they can be properly supported, because the dislocation will quickly return.
And it is reduced in this other way: which is that a long pole be stuck into the ground with its top being round like a pestle, neither thick nor thin, and nearby it should be placed below the patient; then when you place over the top of the pole lion’s skins, and the patient stands according to the length of the pole, and then his hand is stretched out behind at the sides; and his body from the other side is extended with strength, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
And if the reduction of it is difficult due to all the causes we have mentioned, then use this treatment: which is that you take a pole whose length is two cubits, and its width is four fingers, and the thickness of it should be proportioned according to the size of the thimble, as follows:
And then over the round top place lion’s skins so that the pole does not hurt the patient.
And then it should be placed below the joint of the patient, and his hand or arm should be stretched over the pole behind at the sides, and the pole should be wrapped around the helper’s arms and the extremity of the hand.
Then place the arm on a step of a ladder according to its width, and the hand should be stretched out behind at the sides, and the other body part should be suspended from the other side, for the joint will return quickly.
And when the instrument of it is completed according to each way that is possible for you, then place below the joint a pad of wool of equal thickness; and then apply a plaster prepared from powdered myrrh and egg white over all the muscle, as mentioned above, and then bind tightly below the joint with proper pressure; and the bandage should be wrapped around the plaster, and his hand should be attached to his neck, and left alone, and do not disturb his hand for seven days.
When the patient's food has been restricted for a short time, so that the limb is strengthened, it is easier for recovery to occur.
And when the food is given more frequently, every seven or five days, and one movement is exercised; and thus if it is fixed and not softened, then it is healed.
If the joint is dislocated due to swelling that occurs because of moisture or for some other reason, then it should be treated with cautery three times, as was stated earlier in the chapter on cautery.
And if all this is done and after seven days the bandage is removed and the joint is not fixed, and the plaster and the ligature are reduced several times, but it is not fixed and the hand cannot raise that arm to its usual position, then you will know that the nerves of it, which run alongside the muscle, have been cut or stretched or softened, and understand that the joint will never be set in place again.
Regarding the dislocation that occurs due to the feet and the heel or the rear parts, the reduction is done with impulse and extension as much as necessary until it returns to place.
And the other ligature should be applied to it, and care for it until it is healed.
And if there is stiffness and delay in the movement of the limb as it recovers, then the patient should use baths several times until that stiffness is alleviated and returns to its original nature.
Chapter XXVII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the collarbone.
Know that the joint of the collarbone is dislocated with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated in all directions, but more so towards the front than behind. And this dislocation does not hide from you as it appears below either through vision or touch according to its figure, and when you adjust the dislocated bone, that is clearly declared to you, for the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm nor reach the shoulder muscle.
And you should treat it, and reduce the dislocation as soon as possible before it becomes infected, for if it becomes infected, the reduction of the bone is difficult, and even then it may not heal completely; especially when it is in the rear positions, for they are worse than all other types of dislocations, more painful, and many people have died because of them.
And the restoration of it, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the hand be extended towards the two sides, and the arm be expanded, and the physician's hands should be over the collarbone behind it, and he should push the joint with his fingers together or with the heel of his palm until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is towards the front, then it returns as he bends his hand with his other hand, until he strikes the collarbone with the heel of his palm.
And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer vigorous extension and strong movement.
And it is that two assistants extend the arm, and hold it firmly by two ministers so that it does not move during the extension.
After you have turned the arm completely around, since it is wrapped in a long doubled cloth over the hand, or bandage.
And when the surgeon touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil so that this may be those causes which help in the lubricity of the joint and ease; and then press the joint firmly inward until it returns.
And after the reduction of the shoulder, place it on a plaster which has styptic and protective properties over an egg membrane, and bind it with moderate tightness, and hang the arm from the neck, and leave it like this for several days.
And then it should be loosened; and if the joint is formed in its proper place, now you undo the bandage of it and leave it.
And if you see the joint not properly strengthened, then replace the plaster and bandage, and leave it like this for several days until it is healed; and then loosen it.
But if it happens that there is swelling around the reduction of the joint, and its position is in movement, then administer moistening by bath and gentle friction, and compression until it is calmed; or place cold applications on the joint, which are suitable for excessive motion; and then bind it, and leave it on the bone day and night. And then remove that and put it in water; when dry, now rub the joint with moderate friction, and then apply cold applications again, which are suitable for excessive motion, once, and the second time, and the third time. Take an instrument for bathing, ensure that it is clean.
And if you wish, place a dressing over that moist and inflamed wound, and apply it several times, since it is moist, and reduce it to its proper condition.
Chapter XXVIII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the elbow joint, which is between the arm and the hand. Note that there seems to be a minor error or inconsistency in the phrase "between the arm and the hand," as typically the elbow joint is between the upper arm and the forearm. However, I have translated it as closely as possible to the original text.
When the hand is dislocated in some cases, the reduction of this dislocation is easy, but the opposite is true for other joints. However, it is important that there be speed in reducing it at the moment when it is dislocated, before swelling occurs or an abscess forms.
The reduction of this dislocation is done by placing the joint of the hand of the injured person on a table and having an assistant extend the hand. The surgeon then places their hand or palm over the vicinity of the joint and presses it until it returns to its proper place.
However, it is important to consider whether the palm is already displaced towards the back of the hand. If this is the case, the injured person should place the back of their hand on a table during extension and reduction. If the dislocation is towards the back of the hand, then the position of the hand should be directly on the table so that the surgeon's hand can press over the vicinity of the joint.
And if it returns quickly, that is good; but if not, then dress it with a bandage to allow for swelling and leave it as such. Do not repeat this, because otherwise, the patient will suffer and you cannot proceed with the reduction until more days have passed.
However, the joint remains stiff according to its curvature; and you find in the patient no other cause, except that the muscle is softened and cannot be strengthened by any means; now you know that the nerves are cut or torn, and now it does not heal there unless it is made firm with cauterization and then some cause is conferred upon it.
Then when the inflammation has subsided, now pause on the bandage which we have recommended, and let it be left and strengthened for five days; and afterwards let it be loosened and left, and let the arm be exercised.
However, if the movement of it remains and you encounter any gravity in it, now treat that with warm water and some friction several times, until it is alleviated.
Chapter XXIX.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the said joints.
In some cases, the said joints are dislocated completely.
And when any of them is dislocated towards the thumb or to the palm side, now press the finger and push the dislocation with your fingers until it returns.
And then support the tip of the finger, and suspend that from the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
And then suspend that and extend it until it is properly healed on its own.
And when it is night, wrap that according to the mentioned narration, and do not stop loosening it during the day and exercising with movement, and wrap it at night; and do this for several days until it is strong.
If it appears that the joint is dislocated at the elbow, and he ties it to that part and does with it the first operation, until it is healed.
And similarly do this when it is dislocated in other parts.
Chapter XXX.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When a complete dislocation occurs in any of the vertebrae of the back or neck and the vertebrae are moved from their places, there is no treatment at that moment, for death is expected by the patient.
The sign of this is that the excretion from the patient occurs without their will and they cannot retain it; often some parts of them or both legs or arms, or one of them are weakened; or a vertebra is moved from its place, as sometimes it moves and at other times the displacement goes to four directions.
When it is displaced towards the rear parts, it is called retrolisthesis.
However, the treatment for this is to determine whether the retrolisthesis occurs due to weakness, because in that case there is no cure and it will not heal completely.
But in the case where it occurs due to falling or impact or something similar to these, the Ancients described the methods of treatment at length, which I have summarized for brevity; however, little excuses the excess with my proximity to understanding and exposition, in which I have laid out this and formed it from various instruments different from those used to demonstrate this and explain that.
And of those, it is not cured except that which occurs in the two properly as I recount.
And it is that the patient lie extended on his face upon a level surface near a wall, and be stretched out below the bed moistened with something so that the feet are not lifted.
And nearby a straight post should be placed, inserted into the ground at the end of the level surface near his head, and another post around his feet at the other end of the level surface.
And the minister hold the post, and it should not be fixed in a hole; and another minister hold the other according to that form.
And nearby wrap around the head of the patient and below the shoulders tie a firm bandage; and extend the end of the bandage to the post which is behind his head, and tie that to it; and then stretch the other bandage over the hips of the patient with the knees, and behind the buttocks; and then adjust all the bandages and tie those to the other post which is behind his feet.
And the minister should extend the bandage to the side of the post, and do not move the two posts and the bandages from their places in which they are fixed, but rather ensure they are not moved as we have said.
And the physician should place his palms on the spine with strength until it returns, or he places upon that a table, and then adjust the patient's feet on the table until it returns.
If this method does not return the desired result, take a table whose width or length is about three cubits and dig near where we said the patient should be a place into which the end of the table can fit. And then place the middle of the table over the spine, and the surgeon places his feet on the other end, and gently presses until the spine is compressed and returns to its proper place.
And if you wish, open again the one that is wrapped around the hand; and place the patient's head at the end of two poles, the length of which is one arm, and behind his head place another pair of poles in the same manner; and the distance between each pole should be a palm, and attach ropes to each pole through which they are firmly secured to the ground so that they do not move.
And place one round pole in which is the rope, and let it be wrapped around the two ropes of the two poles.
And at the end of it is a rope in which the pole is attached, its length being one palm including what is wrapped around; and on the other two poles there is something similar to that.
And then pull the ropes that you have tied to the patient's feet at the rope behind his head, and the ropes that you have tied to the patient’s waist at the rope behind his legs.
And then place someone behind each rope who will turn the rope with their hand, and the surgeon will adjust the spine as we have said.
And after the spine is returned to its place and the area is leveled, it is necessary to apply an excrescent plaster with honey on alum, and then cover it with wool; and afterwards place over the plaster a strip of wood whose width is three fingers or around that size, and the length should be as much as is needed for the diseased area, and on both sides of the spine.
And afterwards apply the bandage we have mentioned, and use a mild diet until he is healed.
And if there is any inflammation in the area at the end of the healing process, it is necessary to administer medicines that reduce and soothe, along with prolonged application of the board we have recommended; and already a lead plate should be applied to that location.
And at times inflammation occurs in the lower part of the spine, and it is caused by that dislocation; and that bone is one where excessive inflammation can lead to death if too much precaution is not taken with this medicine.
Chapter XXXI.
On the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the muscle attachment are subject only to dislocation, and do not suffer what happens to other joints with slight movement and concavity.
And the joint of the hip can be dislocated in four ways, as it can be dislocated inward, outward, forward, or backward.
And it is more severely displaced towards the interior parts, and somewhat towards the anterior or posterior parts.
The sign of the dislocation towards the interior parts is that when you adjust the limb of the healthy person next to the affected one, it appears longer and the knuckle more prominent than in the healthy side, and the patient cannot bend their leg even slightly, as this location where bending occurs is obstructed by inflammation or abscess due to cautery, so the head of the bone has already reached here.
The sign of the one to whom dislocation occurs towards the exterior parts is that the symptoms are contrary to those described for this condition.
The sign of the one to whom dislocation occurs towards the anterior parts is that the limb is extended but cannot be bent without pain in the joint, and if they attempt to walk, they are unable to do so towards the front; and their urine is retained and their swallowing is obstructed by an abscess, and they must walk on their heel.
The sign of the one to whom dislocation occurs towards the posterior parts is that they cannot extend the joint, nor can they bend it forward as they would normally bend their neck, and the limb is shorter than the other, and the throat is softened, and the head of the bone is in that location.
However, the reduction of these types of dislocation is that you consider whether the dislocation is old and ancient, in which case it has already settled in its position, so you administer reduction according to its disposition, but at this point it is not a complete cure; therefore, there should be no expectation for an immediate recovery.
In the case of the one in which the dislocation is recent, and it is one of the four species of dislocation, place your hand so that you turn the joint and extend it to the spaces within and to the spaces without, and move it to the right and left, for it easily returns and does not require other treatment.
If it does not return in this manner, then you must prepare a strong minister and extend the hollow from beneath the part below, or insert your fingers there, or apply a bandage that lies over the hollow above the denol; and another minister should extend the hand from above such that he places his fingers underneath the tubercles of it; and immediately bind with a lion bandage over the root of the joint, and have another minister hold the extremity of the bandage.
And this extension of it or of the part within to the angle of the shoulder blade, or of the posterior part to the part of the back.
And this extension of all those at once within, while the patient is raised upon his body from the ground and remains hanging.
And this species of extension is common to all four.
And thus if the dislocation returns as we have said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary for all species what I shall say about its proper treatment.
In the reduction of it when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is to the inner parts, the patient should be reclined on his side; then place the bandages over the root of the joint at the location where the head of the joint and the place behind the angle meet; then extend the bandage from above the part of the angle to the most posterior part of the shoulder blade.
Let another strong minister take the arms of the patient and pull the place around the diseased bone strongly to the sides through extension, for that will return it to its proper place.
And this species is the one that heals most quickly among those to which this member is reduced compared to the others.
But if it swells up on you and does not obey the treatment in this species of healing for a long time, then now it is necessary to bind both sides of the patient together with strong, quick bandages over both heels and both toes.
And let the length of each band be the quantity of four fingers, and let the diseased limb be extended more than the other by a quantity of two fingers.
And let the patient be suspended at the top of the post, and hold it in their hand; and let the distance from the ground be the length of two arms.
And then command a strong youth to pull the head of the bone, and let another youth hold the patient, while this other youth pulls the limb with great force, for the joint will return to its place quickly.
But in the proper reduction, when it is dislocated to the outer part, let the patient lie on a bench as we have recommended for the yellow jaundice; and bind the bandage tightly around the affected limb and the feet.
And then place two posts, one at the foot of the patient and the other at the head; and then place adjusted posts on each side of the pillar on which damp cloths are wrapped, so that the patient is not damaged by the post being between his limbs, to prevent the extension downward from being pulled.
Let each servant extend his part, and the physician apply his skill to reduce it according to art.
And when the dislocation is to the front parts, then it is necessary that the limb be extended quickly, and according to this narration mentioned above for the back; and the physician places the palm of his right hand on the affected area, and with one hand pulls that tight, and while pulling tightly extends that towards the lower part from the front.
And if the dislocation is to the rear parts, then it is necessary that the patient be extended towards the lower part and raised off the ground; however, it is necessary that he be paused on a hard surface, just as it is necessary for the same thing if the hip joint is dislocated outward, according to what we have said about the positioning of the body over two supports; and it is on his face with the limbs extended, as we have said, there is no harm.
And it is necessary to apply compression to the table at the location of the joint.
Therefore, when the reduction is completed as necessary — and the sign of hip joint reduction is not hidden from you, which is that you extend both legs of the patient, and when you see them equal and the patient extends his leg freely without discomfort, then you know that the limb has returned to its proper place — and now you must adjust two things: apply a bandage and wrap it so that the hip joint is not moved in any direction; and instruct the patient to rest for three or four days.
Then undo the bandage and the dressing, and compare one side with the other.
Now, if you see those marks on the body, now you know that the dislocation has been fixed, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any sign of inflammation, now you return and apply a plaster to it, and treat it according to your initial structure, and let it be for three days.
Then undo that [treatment], and have the patient delay in walking on each [limb] for several days, until they are comfortable.
Chapter XXXII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the joint.
The joint can be dislocated in three ways, as it can be dislocated outward, inward, and to the rear rather than forward; it cannot be dislocated forward.
And the sign of dislocation is that you command the patient to apply their hand to the chest; now you know that the joint is dislocated.
And the restoration of all types of dislocation is to instruct the patient to straighten their limb as much as possible; then place your hand against the side of it, and put your feet between his foot; then attach the joint with both your palms, and hold them firmly over the joint with strength, and another assistant should extend the leg until the joint returns to its proper place.
And the sign of successful reduction is that the patient's hand adheres to the chest effortlessly and naturally.
Apply the plaster to it and make the cup adhere to the wound, then bind those with a bandage for three days or four, and afterward loosen them.
And do not use the limb for walking for several days until it is healed and comfortable.
But if the reduction of it to you is unnecessary, now administer a wide extension with bandages, as previously described in the treatment of the hip.
Chapter XXXIII.
On the treatment of talus dislocation.
The talus sometimes is displaced by slight movement, and sometimes it is dislocated completely, and the dislocation of it occurs either towards the interior parts or towards the exterior parts.
And the sign of its dislocation is that the talus becomes inflamed towards the part to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment for its displacement is gentle reduction, and it should be extended with ease using bandages until it returns to its normal position.
But in the treatment when it is completely dislocated, ensure that the limb is straight, and have a strong and capable assistant hold it behind both of them at the midpoint.
And you should hold with your right hand the most superior part of the foot, and with your left hand the part below the heel at the location of the ankle.
And then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and with your left hand do it twice, and then extend with your left; and press the toes of the foot with your right hand around the cup with effort.
In this place two incisions as we have counted.
And then press the feet of the patient to the third incision, and you will be at the nail.
Therefore, if it returns in one incision or in two according to this method, and if the foot heals properly, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation, for that is what happens.
But if you are prohibited from reducing the deformity as we have recommended, then lay the patient on their back on the ground, and place a board firmly on the ground under them, so that it fits between their thighs, and you should wrap a cloth around them so that they do not displace the injury.
And then the attendant should hold the patient's thighs, and another attendant should extend the injured foot with their hands or with bandages around the neck of the foot; and then each attendant should pull against the extension as instructed by them, and the board should be placed between the patient’s thighs to retain it, so that the body does not be drawn towards parts below the desired extension.
And then the surgeon should heal the dislocation with his hands, and another attendant should hold the healthy thigh below, as the dislocation will return with ease.
And when the dislocation returns and you are assured of the successful reduction, now apply a plaster and bind it firmly with bandages, and secure the foot with bandages to the board.
And be careful to avoid the nerves which are located behind the heel, so that the bandage over them is not too tight and does not injure them severely.
Let it remain like that for two days or three; and if the dressing is softened, remove it.
Then untie that on the third day or on the fourth, and prohibit the patient from moving for forty days. But if he is forced to move before that time, there is no assurance that the dislocation will not be dissolved and corrupted, and then it will not admit of cure.
And if it happens that the abscess is cauterized, you should now apply what we have previously described concerning the administration of the liquid for treatment and application in another place until it is healed.
Chapter XXXIV.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the said limbs.
It is necessary that the one who is dislocated be kept at rest for a short period without work; and what is not difficult is quickly accomplished.
But if the dislocation is in some joints of the leg, then you should have the patient lie on his back on a level surface of the ground or on a table, and let him be positioned as if he were sitting.
Then stand on your foot, and place your other foot above the joint you intend to treat, and then step down with force until it returns to its normal position, so that you see it become aligned and no longer protrude in an elevated location.
Then place the bottom of the foot on a board that covers the entire foot, which has two ends, and then bind it securely with proper bandaging for three days; then untie it and allow the patient to rest from walking for ten days until it is healed and recovery is assured.
Chapter XXXV.
Concerning the kinds of dislocations that are made due to plague or fracture or any such conditions.
When any cause of those occurs, and you proceed with the treatment of it and the restoration of it, several times death may succeed.
And because of this, one should not presume to treat it unless they are wise in the art, experienced for a long time, merciful and pious, neither hasty nor presumptuous; and at the beginning use medicines that only reduce swellings, and leave the patient to divine judgment, but rather to whom you hope will bring salvation from destruction, or relief from illness, where some hope appears. And then strive to immediately perform the reduction of it at the beginning of the cause, before it becomes a swollen abscess.
And then if the member returns as we want, immediately administer a regimen that reduces swelling and treats the injury with what is appropriate from among dry ointments.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and occurs in the bones that are separated, then you proceed to set them, and do as we have said regarding simple injuries in the places where those bones are located.
And guard and avoid, according to your virtue, the use of instruments in ways that cause fear as my teachings have instructed you; for this is better for you to serve your Creator, and more permanent for your glory, and safer for your true body, which you will experience in your time. Thus finely are the words of Albucasis perfectly translated from which God be blessed. Amen.
